I tried this with Claude 3 Opus by copying and pasting the entire prompt chain into the box and it worked. The LLM was able to take the steps and develop the story while keeping narrative logic. Pretty amazing, isn’t it?

The prompt chain takes advantage of the LLM’s context as it generates its response.

One of the chains Elizabeth taught is to ask the LLM to analyze a scene, suggest enhancements, and write it (all within the same prompt).

Example of a Prompt Chain in paragraph form:

Upload your scene into the context window and use this prompt. It’s best to tag the scene with brackets. [scene]{contents of scene}[/scene] so that the LLM will know what you’re referring to when you mention scene. Tagging is explained in Chapter 21 Megaprompting.

<prompt>

Identify places in the scene to make the writing stronger, longer, more dialogue, more detailed, better characterization, or less cliche. Make a quick plan on how to add more great writing to the scene. This can be an expansion of dialogue, add minor characters, or interaction with the scene between characters. Your quick plan should only be a few lines of ideas and set off by [rewrite plan] [/rewrite plan].

Please rewrite the scene word for word, exactly as is, but adding in the ideas of the rewrite plan. The final scene must be longer, with more writing and character interaction than the original. Paragraphs, where action is summarized, are a great place to expand with sensory details and emotional drama. Avoid foreshadowing.

</prompt>

​[ ​18​ ]​

NEEDS ASSESSMENT PROMPTING

Sometimes, the best way to provide information to an LLM is to ask what it would need to perform a specific task. Different LLMs may require different prompting styles simply because they were trained on diverse datasets.

Asking the AI what it needs is a technique taught by Elizabeth Ann West. It is called “needs assessment prompting” and involves engaging in a dialogue with the AI model to understand its specific requirements for completing a given task effectively. By asking the AI questions about the information it needs, authors can gain insights into how the model thinks, what data it prioritizes, and how to best structure prompts for optimal results.

BENEFITS FOR FICTION AUTHORS

Improved Prompt Design: By understanding the AI’s needs, authors can craft more effective prompts that provide the necessary context, details, and instructions for generating desired outputs.

Enhanced Collaboration: Treating the AI as a partner with specific requirements fosters a more collaborative approach, leading to better communication and understanding between the author and AI.

Reduced Trial and Error: Needs assessment can minimize the need for extensive trial and error when experimenting with prompts, as authors gain a clearer understanding of what information the AI requires upfront.

Increased Efficiency: By tailoring prompts to the AI’s needs, authors can achieve desired results more quickly and efficiently, saving time and effort during the writing process.

Unlocking Creative Potential: Understanding the AI’s capabilities and limitations allows authors to explore its full potential and discover new ways to leverage its power for creative storytelling.

HOW TO CONDUCT NEEDS ASSESSMENT PROMPTING

Define the Task: Clearly identify the specific task you want the AI to perform, such as generating a scene, developing a character, or writing a plot outline.

Formulate Questions: Ask the AI about the information it needs to complete the task effectively. Examples include:

“What kind of information would be helpful for you to write a compelling scene?”

“What details about the characters would you need to create a believable dialogue?”

“What information about the setting would help you generate a vivid description?”

Analyze Responses: Consider the AI’s answers carefully to understand its priorities and requirements. Look for patterns or recurring themes in its responses.

Refine Prompts: Based on the AI’s feedback, refine your prompts to include the necessary information and context.

Iterate and Experiment: Continue the dialogue with the AI as needed, refining your prompts further and exploring different approaches to achieve your desired results.

EXAMPLES OF NEEDS ASSESSMENT PROMPTS

Scene Generation:

“What details about the setting would help you create a vivid description?”

“What information about the characters’ emotional states would help you write a scene with a strong emotional impact?”

“Do you need any information about previous events in the story to ensure continuity within this scene?”

Character Development:

“What kind of background information about the character would be most helpful for you to develop their personality and motivations?”

“Would it be useful to know the character’s goals and desires or their relationships with other characters?”

“Is there anything specific you need to know about the character’s physical appearance or mannerisms?”

Plot Outlining:

“What information about the story’s genre and target audience would help you create a compelling plot outline?”

“To develop a strong plot structure, Do you need to know the main conflict or the protagonist’s goal?”

“Would it be useful to provide information about the desired length or pacing of the story?”

Later on, we’ll see how to ask the AI what it needs from you to write a scene. We’ll assemble these materials into a scene brief, giving the LLM the material it needs to build a focused scene that includes the details you need, the story progression, character motivations, and goals.

Asking the LLM what it needs is a great way to get better results. Incidentally, different LLMs may require you to prompt it differently. You might notice that a prompt that works well in ChatGPT does not give the same level of response from another LLM. In this case, it is best to ask the new LLM what it needs to perform a task you have in mind.

​[ ​19​ ]​

COMPLETION PROMPTING

Completion prompting is quite simple. It is similar to leading by example and passing the baton on to the AI. This simply means typing in context and letting the LLM continue writing. Completion prompting is an excellent way of showing the LLM your writing style, ideas, and content as it continues to write in the same context window.

The dynamic and interactive nature of completion prompting suits discovery writers or pantsers well. The way you do it is to provide the AI with a partially written scene. You can either enter the partial scene without instructions or give it some guidance on what you want to see happen next.

Using this interactive approach, authors can leverage AI’s ability to continue the narrative, generate ideas, explore different directions, and uncover hidden possibilities within their stories.

HOW COMPLETION PROMPTING WORKS

Start Writing: Begin your scene or chapter as you normally would, writing until you need inspiration, want to explore different options, or simply feel stuck.

Feed the AI: Copy and paste your partially written text into the AI prompt window.

Set the Stage: Provide additional context or instructions if necessary. This might include details about the character’s emotions, the setting, or the overall tone of the scene.

Prompt for Completion: Instruct the AI to continue the narrative. You can use simple prompts like “Continue writing” or “What happens next?” or provide more specific instructions based on your desired outcome.

Evaluate and Iterate: Analyze the AI’s response, considering its coherence, creativity, and alignment with your vision. Use this feedback to refine your subsequent prompts, guiding the AI towards the direction you want to take the story.

BENEFITS FOR DISCOVERY WRITERS

Overcoming Writer’s Block: Completion prompting can help you break through moments of stagnation by providing fresh ideas and alternative paths for your narrative.

Exploring Different Possibilities: Use the AI to test out different scenarios, character choices, or plot twists, discovering new directions for your story that you might not have considered otherwise.

Unveiling Hidden Potential: The AI’s responses can reveal unexpected aspects of your characters or plot, helping you uncover hidden potential within your story.

Maintaining Momentum: Completion prompting can help you maintain a consistent writing pace, keeping the story moving forward even when unsure of the next step.

TIPS FOR EFFECTIVE COMPLETION PROMPTING

Provide Enough Context: Ensure the AI has enough information about the characters, setting, and previous events to generate relevant and consistent continuation.

Set Clear Expectations: Use specific instructions to guide the AI toward the desired tone, style, or direction for the continuation.

Experiment with Different AI Models: Each AI model has strengths and weaknesses. Try various options to see which best complements your writing style and generates the most helpful and inspiring responses.

Embrace the Unexpected: Be open to the AI’s suggestions, even if they deviate from your initial plans. Sometimes, unexpected turns can lead to exciting discoveries within your story.

The best part of using AI is actually the surprises. Completion prompting can help you discover insights and directions you hadn’t considered.

You can easily use the chat interface for completion prompting or go to the Playgrounds or developer’s interface, where you can play with the parameters to vary randomness and other factors. See Chapter 24: Playgrounds and Developer Interfaces.

Finally, remember that you are the author and ultimately in control of your narrative. Use the AI’s outputs as inspiration or building blocks, but always feel free to edit, revise, or discard anything that doesn’t align with your vision.

​[ ​20​ ]​

CONTEXT STUFFING

We’ve discussed context windows and how an LLM uses the words inside its context window to determine its response. Now that we have larger context windows, we can provide the AI model with additional information beyond the core prompt. This “stuffed” context helps guide the AI’s responses, ensuring greater accuracy, relevance, and consistency with your desired output.

Giving AI the background material gives you better responses.

BENEFITS OF CONTEXT STUFFING

Improved Accuracy: By providing additional background information, character details, or world-building elements, you can reduce the likelihood of the AI generating inaccurate or irrelevant responses.

Enhanced Relevance: Context stuffing helps ensure the AI’s outputs align with your specific vision for the story, characters, and setting.

Increased Consistency: Providing context about previous events, character relationships, and established lore helps maintain consistency throughout the generated content.

Greater Control over Style and Tone: You can include examples of your desired writing style or specific authors you want the AI to emulate, guiding the tone and voice of the output.

Unlocking Creativity: Context stuffing can spark new ideas and connections within the AI model, leading to more creative and unexpected results.

HOW TO USE CONTEXT STUFFING EFFECTIVELY

Identify Relevant Information: Determine what information is crucial for the AI to understand the context of your prompt and generate the desired output. This might include:

Character profiles and backstories

World-building details and lore

Plot summaries and key events

Genre conventions and tropes

Desired writing style and tone

Structure the Context: Organize the information in a clear and concise manner. Use bullet points, numbered lists, or short paragraphs to effectively present the context.

Integrate the Context into Your Prompt: Include the context before your main prompt or question. You can present it as background information or instructions for the AI. Most of the large-context window chats now allow file uploading.

Experiment with Different Levels of Detail: The amount of context you provide can vary depending on the complexity of your prompt and the desired output. Start with essential information and add more details as needed.

Review and Refine: Evaluate the AI’s responses and adjust the context accordingly. If the output is not aligned with your expectations, refine the information you provide or experiment with different ways of presenting it.

EXAMPLES OF CONTEXT STUFFING

Character Development:

Context: “Luke Sherlock is a cynical detective with a troubled past and a dry sense of humor. He is investigating a murder mystery in a futuristic city filled with advanced technology and corruption.”

Prompt: “Write a scene where Luke Sherlock interrogates a suspect.”

World-Building:

Context: “The story takes place in a medieval fantasy world where magic is outlawed, and those who possess magical abilities are persecuted. The protagonist is a young woman who discovers she has hidden magical powers.”

Prompt: “Describe the magical system of this world and its impact on society.”

Genre and Style:

Context: “I want to write a science fiction story in the style of Isaac Asimov, focusing on the ethical implications of artificial intelligence.”

Prompt: “Write a scene where a human confronts a sentient AI about the future of humanity.”

Context stuffing is a powerful technique for guiding AI models and ensuring they generate outputs that align with your creative vision. By providing relevant information and context, you can unlock AI’s full potential as a writing partner and create richer, more engaging narratives.

This leads us to the technique of Megaprompting, which organizes the content by using tags so that the LLM can later identify and refer to the tagged material.

​[ ​21​ ]​

MEGAPROMPTING

As we’ve seen, stuffing information into a prompt gets us better and more relevant results. This is important for fiction since we are “making things up” and want AI to follow our vision. Of course, with so much information, the next step is to organize it so that subsequent prompts can refer to it.

The folks at the Future Fiction Academy, Elizabeth Ann West, Leeland Atra, and Steph Pajonas, were the first people I knew to use megaprompts, an organized structure for sending information to an LLM.

Unlike traditional, shorter prompts that might offer just a scenario or a single line of inspiration, megaprompts delve deeply into specifics. They can include character backgrounds, setting details, specific conflicts, thematic elements, and stylistic suggestions. However, more important than what they consist of is the nested format that allows the LLM to “organize” the information using labels.

In fiction writing, a megaprompt typically includes various elements that help define the story, characters, setting, and style. Some standard components of a fiction writing megaprompt are:

1. Genre and subgenre

2. Setting details (period, location, atmosphere)

3. Main characters’ profiles (backstory, personality, goals, flaws)

4. Plot outline or story beats

5. Themes and motifs

6. Narrative point of view and tense

7. Tone and mood

8. Stylistic preferences or influences

9. World-building elements (for fantasy or science fiction)

10. Specific scenes or chapters to focus on

By providing a megaprompt, writers refer to the specific information inside the megaprompt so that the AI can use the detailed information in the megaprompt to maintain consistency, develop characters and plot points, and adhere to the specified style and tone.

You can have anything you want in a megaprompt as it is a way of organizing information.

Create a file called Story Information.

[Story Information]

[Title]A Novel Title[/Title]

[Genre]the genre[/Genre]

[Hook]

[/Hook]

[Pitch]

[/Pitch]

[Synopsis]

[/Synopsis]

[Characters]

[Main Characters]

[POV Character]

[/POV Character]

[Antagonist]

[/Antagonist]

[/Main Characters]

[Side Characters]

[/Side Characters]

[/Characters]

[Settings]

[/Settings]

[Story so far]

[/Story so far]

[/Story Information]

Remember that Story Blueprint I talked about? The collection of information from your brainstorming, character, and story development part? You can place them into a nested megaprompt or several flat structures. The point is to provide this information to the LLM in a structured format so you can refer to the tagged information. We use square brackets, but some LLMs like Claude call them XML tags, and they use <tag></tag>. I think it doesn’t matter as long as you are consistent. Therefore, don’t do <doc></document>, as these two tags don’t match.

For example, if you have the following additional information:

[Writing Style]

[/Writing Style]

[Outline]

[Scene 1]

[/Scene 1]

[Scene 2]

[/Scene 2]

…

[/Outline]

Here’s how you use a megaprompt in your chat.

{First, upload the file that contains your megaprompt structured data} [usually, there’s a paperclip icon to upload a file]

<prompt>

Read the [Story Information] and [Outline] and write [Scene 2] using the [Writing Style].

</prompt>

This method allows you to use variables inside your prompt, allowing the prompt to be reused when different megaprompt files are uploaded. What’s even cooler is that the LLMs that are trained with information from August 2023 understand megaprompts in the context of writing fiction. This is because Elizabeth, Leeland, and Steph contributed to forums discussing megaprompts, and this concept has made it into the LLM’s training data.

Try this prompt: Please describe megaprompts in the context of writing fiction.

The LLMs that understand megaprompt, as I described above, as a well-structured container for story-specific information are the following (as of this writing):

claude 3 family: haiku-20240307, sonnet-2024-0229, opus-20240229

gpt-4-turbo-2024-04-09

gemini-pro-dev-api

gemini-pro-1.5

The LLMs that do not understand it in this way are:

gpt4-1106-preview

claude-2.1

command-r-plus

gpt4-0125-preview

mistral-large-2402

The best answer goes to Gemini-pro-1.5:

Megaprompts: Guiding AI with Extensive Context

Megaprompts are a powerful tool in AI-assisted writing, especially for fiction authors seeking to generate longer and more complex narratives with greater control and consistency. A megaprompt is essentially an extensive prompt that provides the AI model with a wealth of information and context, guiding its responses and ensuring they align with your specific vision for the story.

Components of a Megaprompt:

Story Premise and Synopsis: Include a concise summary of your story’s core concept, main conflict, and key plot points. This provides the AI with a roadmap for the overall narrative direction.

Character Profiles: Provide detailed descriptions of your main characters, including their backstories, personalities, motivations, and relationships with other characters. This helps the AI generate dialogue and actions that are consistent with each character’s unique traits.

World-Building Details: If your story takes place in a fictional world, include information about its history, geography, culture, and any unique elements such as magic systems or futuristic technologies. This ensures the AI’s outputs align with the established world-building framework.

Genre Conventions and Tropes: Specify the genre of your story and any relevant tropes or conventions you want the AI to incorporate. This helps the AI generate text that resonates with reader expectations within the chosen genre.

Desired Style and Tone: Provide examples of your desired writing style or specific authors you want the AI to emulate. This helps guide the AI towards producing text that matches the overall tone and voice of your story.

Benefits of Megaprompts:

Increased Accuracy and Relevance: The extensive context provided in a megaprompt helps the AI generate more accurate and relevant responses, reducing the likelihood of inconsistencies or deviations from your vision.

Enhanced Consistency: By providing detailed information about characters, settings, and plot points, you ensure that the AI’s outputs remain consistent throughout the narrative.

Greater Control Over Output: Megaprompts allow for more precise control over the style, tone, and direction of the AI-generated text, ensuring it aligns with your creative goals.

Unlocking Creativity: The rich context provided in a megaprompt can spark new ideas and connections within the AI model, leading to more creative and unexpected outputs.

Efficiency for Longer Narratives: Megaprompts are particularly useful for generating longer narratives, such as novellas or novels, as they provide the AI with the necessary context to maintain coherence and consistency over extended text formats.

How to Create Effective Megaprompts:

Gather Your Story Information: Collect all relevant details about your story, including character profiles, world-building notes, plot outlines, and genre specifics.

Structure the Information: Organize the information in a clear and concise manner, using bullet points, headings, or separate sections for each element.

Combine Elements: Integrate the different components of your megaprompt into a single document or text file.

Iterative Refinement: Test your megaprompt by generating text with the AI and evaluate its effectiveness. Refine the content and structure of the megaprompt based on the AI’s responses.

Tools for Working with Megaprompts:

LLMs with Large Context Windows: Models like Claude 2.0, GPT-4 (32k), and Gemini Pro 1.5 offer larger context windows that can accommodate the extensive information provided in megaprompts.

Prompt Sequencing Tools: Platforms like Future Fiction Academy’s Rexy feed megaprompts to the AI sequentially, maintaining context and ensuring a coherent narrative flow.

Megaprompts offer a powerful tool for fiction authors seeking to harness the full potential of AI in their writing process. By providing extensive context and detailed instructions, you can guide the AI to generate longer and more complex narratives that align with your creative vision, ultimately saving time and unlocking new storytelling possibilities.

Join the Future Fiction Academy ⁠1 to see how megaprompts are used. Gemini is right. It’s much easier to use megaprompting in a tool like Rexy than to continuously copy and paste into a chatbox or upload them as files. Rexy is integrated with a content management system, Notion, that allows you to store your megaprompts as Notion pages. Your prompts can refer to these pages, and Rexy can then send the prompt sequences with megaprompts to the LLM. Results are then written back to your Notion pages.

Here is a video from Steph Pajonas explaining megaprompting. YouTube: AI + MegaPrompts = Fiction MAGIC! (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZCyQkzKZ9Jo) ⁠2

​[ ​22​ ]​

PROMPT SEQUENCING

Gathering information into megaprompts and being able to refer to the information as variables opens the door to programmatic prompting or prompt sequencing. This technique is the brainchild of Elizabeth Ann West and the Future Fiction Academy. The general idea is the following:

Even though we stuffed context into a megaprompt, the LLM works much better if you tell it to focus on a single task. For example, don’t tell it to produce a plot and invent characters simultaneously. You’ll get some “half-ass” interesting results.

Here are the steps:

Decide on the sequence of prompts (tasks) you want done.

Prime the first prompt by uploading global story information, writing style, synopsis, and character list in a megaprompt.

Perform the prompt and concatenate the output from the previous prompt as input to the following prompt.

This ingenious method lets the LLM keep narrative logic because the output of the previous prompt in the sequence goes into the context window for the subsequent prompt. Initially, the AI is primed by the Story Information Megaprompt, but as each step progresses, it also gains information about what was written and can continue with that knowledge for subsequent prompts, thus building up its knowledge store. Note: You must use a large-context window LLM, and these prompt sequences can be expensive since you pay for tokens in and tokens out.

Here is a walkthrough of prompt sequencing with Future Fiction Academy’s Rexy Word Banger. Note: it is possible to sequence prompts outside of Rexy, but it involves a lot of copying and pasting, as well as opening new conversations to clean the context window.

Rexy’s method of loading megaprompts from Notion, as well as fresh API calls per prompt means you can precisely control the data given to the LLM.

1. Prompts and Steps:

Prompts: You create individual prompts that define specific actions or instructions for the AI. These prompts can range from generating scene briefs to writing scenes, stepping through an outline, critiquing written scenes, and enhancing them. The possibilities are limited only by your imagination.

Steps: You organize your prompts into a sequence of steps, representing the desired execution order. This allows you to build a logical flow for the narrative and ensure continuity between different sections of the generated text.

Big Bang Box: The output of each step is appended to the Big Bang Box, which is added to the input context window. You can bracket this box with separate prompts. The “top” prompt is sent to the LLM before the contents of the Big Bang Box, and the “bottom” prompt is appended after the contents. You may also skip or reset the Big Bang Box after selected steps.

2. Customization and Control:

LLM Selection: Rexy Word Banger offers a wide selection of LLMs, including GPT models, Claude, OpenRouter.ai models, and various open-source options. For each step in your sequence, you can choose the AI model that best suits your writing style and genre preferences. This means you can select a different AI model for each prompt.

Parameter Adjustment: You can fine-tune the parameters of each LLM within the sequence, controlling aspects like temperature, top-p, and response length. This level of control allows you to tailor the AI’s output to your specific needs and preferences. See Chapter 26: Playground Parameters.

Context Management: Rexy Word Banger enables you to manage the context window effectively by specifying how much of the previous output should be included in subsequent prompts (by controlling whether the Big Bang box is reset or skipped). This helps maintain coherence and ensures the AI remains aware of past events and character development but also keeps the context window relevant for the steps in the sequence.

3. Iteration and Refinement:

Real-time Feedback: As the AI generates text for each step, you can evaluate the output and adjust your prompts or parameters if you step through the bangs one at a time. You may edit what is in the Big Bang Box so that any mistakes or things you don’t want to carry over to the next step are deleted or fixed. This iterative process allows continuous refinement and ensures the narrative progresses in the desired direction.

Branching Paths: Rexy Word Banger allows you to create branching paths within your prompt sequence, exploring alternative storylines or character choices. This feature is handy for discovery writers or authors who want to test different possibilities before committing to a specific direction. You can do this by reloading the content management “Notion” slots with alternate material, such as the story information, writing style, or a different outline, or you can edit the Notion slots or Word Banger boxes and run them ad-hoc.

Easily Reproducible Sequences: Word Banger also allows you to reproduce the exact prompts, information, and sequences but rerunning with different LLMs, parameters, or settings. This will enable you to compare and contrast multiple versions of your output.

4. Additional Features:

Notion Integration: Rexy Word Banger seamlessly integrates with Notion, allowing you to organize your story information, character profiles, and world-building details within your Notion workspace.

Custom Templates: You can create and save custom templates for different types of scenes, character interactions, narrative structures, and anything you want. These are called 21 Blackjacks, a database of prompt sequences, but are not limited to 21 prompts. It’s just a fun name.

Collaboration Tools: Rexy Word Banger offers features for sharing your prompt sequences and collaborating with other writers through Notion sharing and duplication.

Benefits of Prompt Sequencing with Rexy Word Banger:

Precision and Control: Edit and rewrite the AI’s output to match your specific vision for the story before putting it back into the next prompt.

Narrative Coherence: Maintain consistency and continuity throughout your narrative.

Creative Exploration: Experiment with different storylines, character choices, world-building elements, writing styles, LLMs, etc.

Efficiency and Productivity: Streamline your writing process and generate content more quickly.

Process Flexibility: Works for either pantsers or plotters or in between.

Collaboration and Sharing: Collaborate with other writers and share your prompt sequences.

Note: Access to Rexy Word Banger requires a membership in the Future Fiction Academy. ⁠1 (They do, however, have a Rexy-only option without the workshops and clubs).

Future Fiction Academy has several YouTube Live videos that demonstrate Rexy Wordbanger. My favorite one is where Elizabeth Ann West writes a Christmas story. Rexy Clause Write In (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Vve5qct4Vyo) ⁠2

​[ ​23​ ]​

MULTIMODAL PROMPTING

At the beginning of last year, we were happy that we could send and receive text messages from ChatGPT. Near the end of the year, OpenAI introduced multimodal prompting into GPT-4, which made conversing with ChatGPT even more exciting.

Multimodal means you can upload an image to the LLM and ask it to describe it using words. Conversely, with the integration of Dall-E image generation, you can now tell ChatGPT-4 to take text and turn it into an image.

Multimodal prompting opens exciting possibilities for fiction authors. If you have trouble describing something, find a photo, upload it to the LLM, and ask it to write a description. Or, if you want to visualize your story world, copy and paste your description into the LLM and ask it to generate an image. You can even upload a book cover, a photograph, or a piece of art and ask the LLM to create a story or suggest a title or genre. You never know what inspiration can come up from mixing images and text.

HOW MULTIMODAL PROMPTING WORKS

Combining Text and Images: Instead of relying solely on text-based prompts, authors can now include images as part of their input to the AI model. This allows the AI to process visual information alongside textual instructions, resulting in richer and more nuanced outputs.

Image-to-Text Generation: AI models can generate text descriptions based on images, providing detailed and evocative descriptions of scenes, characters, or objects.

Text-to-Image Generation: Authors can use text prompts to generate images corresponding to their descriptions, creating visual representations of characters, settings, or key moments in the story.

EXAMPLES OF MULTIMODAL PROMPTING

Setting Description:

Image Input: Upload a photograph of a bustling cityscape at night.

Text Prompt: “Describe the atmosphere of this city, including the sights, sounds, and smells.”

Character Description:

Image Input: Generate an image of a mysterious woman with flowing black hair and piercing green eyes.

Text Prompt: “Describe this woman’s personality and background.”

Plot Development:

Image Input: Generate an image of a spaceship crash-landing on a desolate alien planet.

Text Prompt: “Write a scene about the aftermath of this crash and the challenges the survivors face.”

TOOLS FOR MULTIMODAL PROMPTING

Multimodal LLMs: Models like Google’s Gemini 1.5 Pro and OpenAI’s ChatGPT Plus offer multimodal capabilities, allowing you to combine text and images within your prompts.

Image Generation Tools: Platforms like Midjourney, DALL-E 2, and Stable Diffusion can generate images from text descriptions.

Google’s Gemini 1.5 Pro can even describe videos and transcribe audio files.

Video generation is right around the corner with OpenAI’s demo of Sora, which can generate short videos from text input.

​[ ​PART 4​ ]​

ADVANCED LLM TECHNIQUES

​[ ​24​ ]​

PLAYGROUNDS AND DEVELOPER INTERFACES

By now, almost everyone is familiar with ChatGPT or one of the other chatbots, such as Anthropic’s Claude, Microsoft’s Bing Chat, or Google’s latest entry, Gemini. They are easy to use and widely available.

However, there are reasons for exploring the “playground” or programmer’s interface. Because chatbots are designed for mass consumption, they contain additional programming to filter your input and output to maintain the conversational nature. In my experience, they don’t return as many words as going directly to the LLM’s “Playground” Interface. This is because you have no control over how it manages the chatbot’s context window. Furthermore, the output length is prespecified by the app and not available for you to control as it is when you go through an API or to the playground/workbench.

What is a playground, you ask?

A playground is a developer tool that allows developers to enter text and tweak parameters and other settings. Some even allow you to turn off its “safety” features and allow unfiltered responses.

OpenAI Playground (https://platform.openai.com/playground)⁠1, where you can access current models as well as “legacy” modes, including completion and “beta” modes. There’s also a compare mode that allows you to give the same prompt to two different models. Note: these modes are subject to change.

Claude Workbench (https://console.anthropic.com/workbench)⁠2 provides access to current and older models. It also includes the ability to “get code” for the prompts you’ve written and upload images. You have control over token-length in the response.

Google Gemini Pro (https://aistudio.google.com/)⁠3 includes the ability to upload multimodal content and tweak parameters. The AI studio allows you to edit “Safety,” which controls the degree of content filtering. They also allow you to save your prompts or chats to Google Drive.

OpenRouter is an LLM aggregator (https://openrouter.ai/playground)⁠4 with a playground they call “Chat.” It allows you to load several models and prompt them at the same time. You can also tweak and adjust parameters for each model you are running, and you can export your chat to save it.

Because these playgrounds, workbenches, or studios are developer interfaces, their features vary based on what they can do and what is provided to the user. It’s best to read the documentation of each playground. We’ll go over some commonalities in the next section.

​[ ​25​ ]​

SYSTEM, USER, ASSISTANT BOXES

The first thing you might notice in most playgrounds are the different boxes: System, User, and Assistant, or System, User, Message, or System Instruction, Chat.

SYSTEM INSTRUCTION

Let’s talk about System Prompt or System Instruction first. Remember when we did Role-Based Prompts, we told the LLM to take on a persona in order to better serve the user? And then we had to remind the LLM of the role it was playing every so often.

System Prompts or System Instructions set the background information of the session. They apply to every interaction until you change them and can be useful for defining roles, setting formats and rules, or steering the model’s behavior. They can also provide external information. For example, when I used Playground to generate scene text, I would put the Story Information Megaprompt in the system box and then the “Write {scene beat}” in the user box, plugging in one beat at a time. The output would show up in the Assistant or Message box.

I suspect that ChatGPT’s Custom Instructions were a way of introducing System Instruction to the chat interface, giving more context and instructions to the conversation.

USER PROMPT

The user prompt is the part of the prompt that usually goes into the chatbox. It is the step by step instruction or question that we’re familiar with. It is the prompt you type in or enter.

For examples of how to use System and User prompts, take a look at the Claude Prompt Library. https://docs.anthropic.com/claude/prompt-library ⁠1

Notice that the “Storytelling Sidekick” uses a System Prompt to give the LLM a role, and a User Prompt to give it a specific task.

Meanwhile, the “Corporate Clairvoyant” puts information into the System Prompt to give the LLM background.

The “Simile Savant” skipped the System Prompt and only uses a User Prompt. This is the default mode when we talk to a Chatbot.

ASSISTANT OR MESSAGE

This is the response from the LLM. One thing that’s great in OpenAI’s Playground, is that you can edit the Assistant’s response before you run the next user prompt. This means you can change anything you don’t like in the response rather than “prompt” the LLM to change what you don’t like (as you have to do in the chat interface). Many times, the chat will refuse to answer due to its filters. However, if you are in Playground and you change the Assistant Message, you can steer the LLM in the direction you want it to go. This is one powerful way to use the playground to develop stories because it gives you more control over the contents in the LLM’s context window.

CAVEATS

Developer interfaces are usually not very “user-friendly.” They have knobs and boxes that are not clearly explained. For example, it is not clear how you can save your “chats” or view the history. There may be a little “counterclockwise arrow surrounding a five-o’clock” near the bottom of the screen that allows you to look at recent prompts. OpenAI Playground only saves conversations for 30 days. Google AI Studio has a “save” button that saves and reloads your conversation from Google Drive. A good idea with using these interfaces is to copy and paste what you want to save to another content management system.

The trade-off is that developer interfaces are like power tools. You can do more than you can in the playpen of a chatbox. You can change the parameters to tune the LLM’s responses and, in some cases (Google AI Studio), delete the content filtering. You can view the code for your prompt and try out new techniques, such as different types of prompting: completion, chat, or more.

Let’s look next at the common parameters you can use to tweak and tune the LLM’s response.

​[ ​26​ ]​

PLAYGROUND PARAMETERS

Many LLM platforms offer playgrounds or workbenches where you can interact directly with the AI model and fine-tune its behavior using various parameters. Here are some key parameters to explore:

Temperature: This parameter controls the randomness of the AI’s output. A higher temperature leads to more creative and unexpected results, while a lower temperature results in more predictable and conservative responses.

Top-P and Top-K: These parameters control the diversity of the AI’s vocabulary. Top-P sets a threshold for the probability distribution of the next word, while Top-K limits the selection to the top K most probable words. In practice, Top-P is more advantageous since it is proportional to the size of the result set.

Frequency Penalty: This parameter penalizes the AI for repeating words or phrases, encouraging it to use a broader range of vocabulary and avoid redundancy.

Presence Penalty: This parameter can prevent the AI from focusing too much on specific topics or characters. It penalizes the presence of a word that already exists in the conversation.

Max-Length: This slider limits the number of tokens in the response. Increasing the number of tokens allows the LLM to give more in-depth responses, whereas moving it down makes the AI give you a concise response—which may sometimes be chopped off. Ensure the max-length size is within the context window limits. Sometimes, when your prompt is huge, you must back off on the max-length slider to fit the prompt into the context window. This is the knob you can use to try and get the LLM to give you more words in its response. If I increase the max-length slider too much, I’ve found that the LLM will repeat itself to fill up the space, even if it has nothing new to say. This happens more frequently when using a fine-tune model.

Note: Claude’s Workbench only gives you access to Temperature and Max Tokens to Sample, which is semantically different from OpenAI’s Max-Length. Apparently it means the maximum number of tokens to output and the slider can only go up to 4000. Even though Claude 3 has a 200,000 context window, most of it goes to the input and the prompt. The max tokens output means we can only get around 3000 words per prompt.

In practice, if you do not control the API call, meaning you’re using the chat mode or someone else’s tool, they could have set the limit much lower, to 500 tokens or 1000 tokens. This is why people complain that Claude is lazy or hardly answered the question. It was trying to fit its response into a small number of words. You can ask for 10,000 words, but you’re not going to get it—even if the context window is able to handle it. Incidentally, a table on Claude’s website shows that all models are limited to 4096 tokens of output, which translates to roughly 3000 words. https://docs.anthropic.com/claude/docs/models-overview ⁠1

If you want the LLM to go a little crazy and not follow instructions, you can slide the temperature up a little—not too much, or you might get gibberish. If you want it to stick to your instructions, decrease the temperature and the TopP.

Playing around with a combination of Temperature and TopP can drastically alter the writing style of an LLM. For example, Leeland discovered a way to get GPT-4 to write like Claude 2.0. He would turn the temperature on GPT-4 to 1.7 and lower the TopP to .5 or .7. This way, GPT-4 would have more randomness in word selection but be limited to only the top 70% or 50% of the more random choices.

If you don’t reduce the TopP, your chances of getting gibberish increases.

When Elizabeth wants the AI to follow her outlines or directions, she reduces the temperature to .5 to .7 and the TopP to .5 to .7.

By the way, the ranges for temperature are different between OpenAI and Claude, so it’s a relative number and not an absolute number.

The Future Fiction Academy ⁠2 offers a free introductory class called “Introduction to AI Basics for Fiction Writing” that covers LLMs, playgrounds, and parameters.

See their YouTube Channel: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=o_7PTSlFNn8&ab_channel=FutureFictionAcademy ⁠3

​[ ​27​ ]​

CONTEXT WINDOW CONSIDERATIONS

Imagine having a conversation with someone who can only remember the last few sentences you said. Frustrating, right? That’s similar to how LLMs operate with their limited context window.

While we’ve already talked about context windows, we didn’t go into detail because most chatbots “hide” context windows from the user. You only notice that the chatbot seems to have forgotten something you told it earlier.

Users also notice that the chats are sometimes terse and never seem to give you the number of words you asked for. This is because you have no control over the context window, whether the chat uses up the window by holding on to more past information or allowing more open space for its response.

The playground or developer’s interface makes the user more aware of the context window because it has a parameter that determines the “max-length” of the prompt and response, so now is a good time to go into more detail on how context windows work.

What is a Context Window?

An LLM’s context window refers to how much text it can consider and “remember” when processing and generating a response. Think of it as the AI’s short-term memory. It holds a limited number of tokens (words or word fragments), including previous prompts and responses, along with the current prompt.

Why is Context Window Size Important?

The context window size directly impacts the AI’s ability to understand complex prompts, maintain narrative coherence, and generate consistent and relevant outputs. A small context window limits the amount of information the AI can process, leading to potential issues such as:

Loss of context: The AI may forget earlier parts of the conversation or story, resulting in irrelevant or contradictory responses.

Repetition: The AI may repeat information or phrases due to limited access to previous text.

Inability to handle complex tasks: Tasks requiring a larger context, such as summarizing long documents or generating detailed narratives, become challenging.

STRUCTURING PROMPTS FOR DIFFERENT CONTEXT WINDOW SIZES

Small Context Windows:

Keep prompts concise and focused: Provide only the most essential information.

Break down complex tasks: Divide tasks into smaller prompts that fit within the window. Instead of prompting for an entire scene, break the prompt down into scene beats.

Use reminder prompts: Reiterate key information to refresh the AI’s memory.

Large Context Windows:

Utilize context stuffing/megaprompts: Provide additional background information, character details, or world-building elements.

Employ prompt chaining: Break down complex tasks into sequential prompts.

Explore complex narratives: Generate longer and more intricate stories with greater continuity.

THE EVOLUTION OF CONTEXT WINDOW SIZE

Early LLMs had limited context windows, often around 2000 tokens (approximately 1500 words). However, recent advancements have led to significant increases in context window size:

GPT-3.5: 4096 tokens (approximately 3000 words)

GPT-4: 8192 tokens (approximately 6000 words)

Claude 3: 200,000 tokens

Gemini Pro 1.5: 1,000,000 tokens

Keep in mind that LLMs charge for both the input and output tokens. Sometimes, the charge is the same, but in most cases, the input tokens cost less than the output tokens.

STRATEGIES FOR EFFECTIVE PROMPTING WITH CONTEXT WINDOWS

Even with larger context windows, it’s crucial to use them efficiently. Here are some strategies:

Prioritize relevant information: Focus on details essential to the current task or scene.

Summarize previous events: To maintain continuity, provide concise summaries of key past events.

Utilize external knowledge bases: Store and reference information in external documents or tools.

Experiment with different AI models: Explore models with varying context window sizes to find the best fit for your needs.

Having a large context window does not always guarantee total recall of information.

RAG AND LARGE-CONTEXT MODELS

Just having a large context window does not automatically guarantee the LLM’s ability to retrieve and relate to every piece of information inside its window. This is because most of the current large-context models, other than Google Gemini 1.5 Pro, use Retrieval-Augmented Generation to summarize data.

In the next chapter, I’ll discuss RAG and suggest ways of getting RAG models to “key” into the information inside our megaprompt that we want it to retrieve and reason on.

​[ ​28​ ]​

RETRIEVAL AUGMENTED GENERATION (RAG)

To understand Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG), we need to understand the mechanisms of how it works.

RAG is a fancy way of saying that the LLM uses external information to help it answer your questions. The LLM scans your text and indexes important words or phrases. This index is like a table of contents that helps it find relevant passages later.

It decides what’s important by using TF-IDF, or “Term Frequency-Inverse Document Frequency.” Imagine you have a book about cats and a book about dogs. TF-IDF would tell you that “meow” is a more important word in the cat book, while “woof” is more important in the dog book.

The index of important words or phrases is stored in a separate database, which is like a library where the AI can quickly access the information it needs.

When you ask a question, the AI uses your question as a search query and compares it to the indexed passages. It finds the passages that are most similar to your question, which is like finding books with similar titles or topics in a library. It then ranks the passages and retrieves the most relevant ones, ordering them by where they appeared in the original text.

Unfortunately, when applied to fiction, RAG often misses important details because it relies on summaries and extracted keywords. It will miss clues, subtle details, or fine plot points that are crucial for a particular story. If a specific fact or event wasn’t deemed “important” during indexing, it may not be retrieved, even if present in the source material.

Since most currently popular large-context models, including Claude 3 and OpenAI GPT-4, utilize RAG, we need to consider strategies for structuring our megaprompts to accommodate RAG.

EFFECTIVE MEGAPROMPTING WITH RAG MODELS

Focus on Key Information: Prioritize including crucial details such as character profiles, major plot points, important settings, and essential world-building elements within your megaprompt.

Structure and Organization: Organize your megaprompt with clear headings, sections, and labels to improve information retrieval. This is why tagging is important. Use consistent keywords in your tags, like <character> <setting> <writing style>.

Utilize Keywords Strategically: Incorporate relevant keywords throughout your megaprompt to increase the likelihood of the AI recognizing and retrieving associated information. Use your characters’ names rather than pronouns. Be repetitive with details you want the AI to retain. For example, if you want snarky dialogue, mention it in the character trait, and then again in writing style, and insert it into the scene brief as a reminder.

Specificity is Key: Avoid vague or ambiguous language. Use precise descriptions and specific terminology related to your story and genre. Name the sword, the pet, and the talisman specifically, and refer to them by their proper names in your prompts.

Multiple Megaprompts: To improve information organization and retrieval, consider creating separate megaprompts for different aspects of your story, such as characters, settings, or plotlines. We’ll talk about this later when dealing with narrative continuity, but you might not want to include all of your previous chapter summaries in your megaprompt. Include only what is relevant.

Utilize Retrieval-Specific Prompts: In addition to providing context, include prompts that explicitly instruct the AI to retrieve specific information from the megaprompt. For example, “Based on the character profile of [Character Name], describe their reaction to this situation…”

Pre-processing and Annotation: Before feeding your text to the RAG model, pre-process it by highlighting or annotating key information, characters, locations, and events by tagging them. For example, if a particular magical item is important for your scene, tag it in your megaprompt and mention it by its tagged name several times.

Complete control over RAG indexing might not always be possible, but you can increase the efficacy of your prompting by tagging and spacing out (repeating) important details in your megaprompts.

​[ ​29​ ]​

CUSTOM INSTRUCTIONS

One of the problems with chatbots is the need to remind the ongoing chat about roles and background information. We’ve already seen how System Prompts, or System Instructions, work across the totality of a conversation. However, they are not available on the chat interface. One way OpenAI did to provide this option is to make a feature called Custom Instructions.

They are available for both the free and the paid versions. You can find the option in your account settings under “Customize ChatGPT.”

It contains two boxes, a top box or a “Preamble” with the question: “What would you like ChatGPT to know about you to provide better responses?”

-AND- a bottom box or Postscript with the question: “How would you like ChatGPT to respond?”

You can enter 1500 characters into each box.

The “Preamble” gives the overall context of the conversation. You can give it a role, or tell the AI what you’re trying to accomplish. For example, development editor, marketing, or brilliant mad scientist fantasy author. The “Postscript” tunes ChatGPT’s response.

I suspect that your “User input” is sandwiched between the “Preamble” box and the “Postscript” box. I don’t know, but Custom Instructions are a great way to alter the way ChatGPT responds to you and give it a role to take on and provide additional information.

The Preamble box conditions the model to go to a particular area of its universe of possible responses. It’s like giving a hint of the domain of words to look for, whereas the Postscript helps it decide what words to return or how it responds.

The Preamble box is added to the top of the prompt and doesn’t seem to affect the output as much as the Postscript which is closer to end of the prompt. Because the large language model skews toward more recently seen words (tokens), the Postscript box has a larger influence in terms of word choices and direction of the responses.

  • Provide Instructions: Enter your desired guidelines and context in the provided text boxes. You can include information such as:

  • Your writing style and preferences: Specify your preferred tone, voice, and level of formality.

  • Your current project: Briefly describe your story’s genre, setting, and main characters.

  • Specific instructions for the AI: Outline what you want the AI to focus on, such as generating ideas, providing feedback, or completing tasks.

Your input is limited because you only have 1500 characters.

Save and Apply: Once you’ve entered your instructions, save them. The AI will now consider these instructions in all your future interactions.

There is a “toggle” at the bottom whether to “Enable for New Chats.” Turn off the Enable if you want to start a chat without the custom instructions.

Update as Needed: As your project evolves or your needs change, revisit and update your custom instructions to ensure they remain relevant.

Benefits of Custom Instructions:

Personalization: Tailor the AI’s behavior to your writing style, preferences, and project requirements.

Consistency: Ensure the AI’s responses remain consistent with your established guidelines and avoid contradictory or off-topic outputs.

Efficiency: Eliminate the need to repeat the exact instructions or provide context in every prompt, saving time and effort.

Targeted Outputs: Guide the AI to generate content that aligns with your specific goals, whether brainstorming ideas, developing characters, or writing scenes.

Custom GPTs have supplanted Custom Instructions for users on the paid account. The free account does not allow access to Custom GPTs, so having a flavor of customization using Custom Instructions is still useful.

If you’re interested in a Writers Room I created using Custom Instructions, please see this webpage “Writer’s Room w/ OpenAI ChatGPT 4” https://adorable-porcupine.super.site/openai-chatgpt ⁠1

Note: Custom Instructions are now available with the free account.

​[ ​30​ ]​

CUSTOM GPTS

ChatGPT Plus offers an exciting feature called Custom GPTs, allowing users to create and utilize AI models tailored to specific domains, writing styles, or individual preferences. It allows the creator to define a set of roles and tasks that the Custom GPT is to do. In addition, the creator can upload files that contain its knowledge base, and advanced creators can even link their Custom GPT to outside data sources. The Custom GPTs are free for users of the paid Plus plan, and there are talks about compensating the designers. Right now, everyone is just putting them up there for free.

You, too, can create your own Custom GPT. Here are the steps.

Name your GPT and select an image, or let Dall-E generate one.

Gather Training Data: Collect a dataset of text that reflects the specific domain, style, or knowledge you want the AI to possess. This could include excerpts from your writing, genre-specific texts, or information about your genre’s world-building, characters, or themes.

Format the Data: You can use XML style tags to delineate your data just like we did with megaprompts. Uploading plain text files is best because it’s easier for the AI to parse. If you upload too much information, your custom GPT’s performance will suffer with spinning wheel syndrome.

Access the Custom GPT Feature: Navigate to the Custom GPT section within your ChatGPT Plus account.

Write out the instructions for what you want the GPT to do.

Upload your knowledge base upon which you want the GPT to draw.

Provide a few sample “prompts” to let users get started.

Test your GPT by prompting it and seeing how it responds.

You may then “publish” it by either making it public and searchable, or for those who have the link, or keeping it private.

Benefits of Custom GPTs for Fiction Writers:

Genre-Specific Expertise: Create Custom GPTs trained on specific genres, such as fantasy, science fiction, romance, or mystery, to ensure the AI’s outputs align with genre conventions and reader expectations.

World-Building Consistency: Train a Custom GPT on your world-building documents and lore to ensure the AI generates text consistent with your established world and its unique elements.

Character Development: Develop a Custom GPT for specific characters, incorporating their backstories, personalities, and dialogue styles to ensure consistent and believable portrayals.

Editing Team: Create a writer’s room of experts, each looking at a different aspect of editing. For example, pacing, tension, dialogue, character arc, scene building, and description.

Using Existing Custom GPTs:

OpenAI offers a community showcase where users can share and access Custom GPTs created by others. This allows you to benefit from domain-specific knowledge and expertise without training your own model from scratch.

An excellent way to approach a custom GPT is to ask what it can do for you. Ask it in different ways, and then ask what information it needs from you. Interview it like you would any job applicant.

Referring to Multiple Custom GPTs within a Chat:

You can access and interact with multiple Custom GPTs within a single ChatGPT chat session by using the “@“ symbol. This pops up the last few Custom GPTs you’ve used or those you’ve pinned. This enables you to leverage the strengths of different customizations and combine their capabilities for even more powerful results.

Among the many exciting possibilities of Custom GPTs is the ability to tailor AI assistance to specific genres. One example is the “Romance Writing Coach,” a custom GPT I designed to help authors write romances. It includes the following knowledge base:

Contemporary Romance Tropes: a list of popular tropes such as “enemies to lovers,” “fake relationships,” and “second chances” to help with premise ideas

Tension Intensifiers: provides guidance on utilizing various tension-building techniques, such as forbidden love, secret pasts, and emotional vulnerabilities, to generate romantic tension.

Romantic Chemistry and Conflicts: offers insights into developing romantic attractions and conflicts, focusing on chemistry despite the obstacles

Dialogue and Banter: This role assists in crafting witty, engaging dialogue and banter that reflects the characters’ personalities and enhances romantic tension.

Character Development: it guides authors in creating well-rounded and likable characters with depth, humor, and emotional resonance.

Scene Crafting and Emotional Impact: helps construct vivid scenes that convey the setting, character interactions, and emotional tone.

Includes a four-act outline that includes the romance-genre plot points and obligatory scenes.

Writing Style and Tone: emphasizes a clear and engaging writing style and utilizes direct, relatable language to convey emotions and character chemistry effectively.

One writer told me she brainstormed and outlined her entire year’s work using Romance Writing Coach. Another friend uses it to brainstorm “what happens next” in her story. I use it for brainstorming premises and characters, and later, when I’m writing, I ask it to generate scene briefs as well as critique and suggest enhancements to my scenes, for example, asking where I can increase romantic tension or enhance chemistry or improve the dialogue to be more snarky.

It’s free to try out if you have a ChatGPT Plus account, and of course, you are not limited to using only one custom GPT. You can call on different ones within the same conversation.

Link to Romance Writing Coach: https://chat.openai.com/g/g-WFzdoMZvY-romance-writing-coach ⁠1

​[ ​PART 5​ ]​

WRITING SCENES WITH AI

​[ ​31​ ]​

USING SCENE BRIEFS

A scene is the essential component of a story. It comprises a setting, character interactions, and significant events. A well-crafted scene is like a short story, with a clear beginning, middle, and end. Characters have specific goals in each scene that they attempt to achieve through their actions and dialogue. These goals can be fulfilled or thwarted by opposing characters or anticipated or unforeseen events. We can look at scenes as a collection of events or “beats” that have a story direction. They are glued together by logical consistency. This happened because of that, and because this happened, something might happen next.

An LLM can assist in writing a scene, but it does not know the specific details of your intended outcome. If you rely solely on the LLM to generate a scene from a sentence in your outline, it may create elements that you did not intend, making it necessary to rewrite or discard most of what was generated. Therefore, providing clear guidance and direction to the LLM is crucial to producing a coherent scene that meets your specific needs.

To do that, we can apply the skills we learned in detailed prompting and the use of megaprompts to give the LLM more context in order to help it deliver a satisfying scene.

We’ll also show how to maintain narrative logic from one scene to the next by providing the LLM of what happened before so that it doesn’t write something that contradicts the story.

While it’s possible to use iterative prompting to tease all of this into the AI, the folks at Future Fiction Academy have figured out an efficient and logical way of using special megaprompts called “Scene Briefs” to provide more context and structure.

Here is one example of a scene brief and what it includes.

Scene type: this could include the genre, such as cozy mystery discovery of the body

Scene goal: the goal of your POV character

Scene setting: a brief description of the setting

Scene characters: who’s POV, what each character wants, and why (list them)

Scene summary: a general description of what’s happening in the scene

Scene beats: a step-by-step sequence of action that directs what is happening throughout the chapter.

The beats should include:

Setup: (optional, or if you want to start in media res, say so)

Inciting Incident for the POV character: something bad or unexpected happens (start here if in media res)

Progression of complications for POV character: (try-fail cycles, either emotional or physical or both, with opposition, conflict, tension)

Scene crisis: doesn’t have to be big, but it must create conflict for the POV character and several options, usually, none good, but of course, if it’s a HEA scene, then yes, make it a win

Scene climax or decision: (omit it if you want a cliffhanger, but you need to pick it up in the next scene with that POV character)

Scene resolution, showing what changed, usually a reversal for the POV character (optional, or hint of what might have changed)

Scene type or style: (is this an action scene or a reaction scene)

Writing tips: (guidance to the LLM on the tone, voice, or style)

Any dialogue snippets: (pieces of dialogue you want to put in your characters’ mouths)

This example is off the top of my head, and you can design your scene brief in any way that works for you. One method taught by the Future Fiction Academy is to ask the LLM what it needs to write a scene. For example, if you are writing a cozy mystery, you can ask the LLM what types of scenes occur in a cozy mystery.

For example, what does it need in the “Discovery of the Body” scene? Consulting books on cozy mystery writing will give you the types of scenes, their goals, emotional tenor, usual suspects, etc.

Let’s ask ChatGPT-4 what it needs to write a “Discovery of the Body” scene.

<Response>

To craft a vivid and engaging “Discovery of the Body” scene for your cozy mystery, here are some details you might provide:

1. Setting Details: Describe the location where the body is found. Is it in a quaint village shop, a bustling local fair, a serene garden, or somewhere within a grand old mansion? Setting details can help set the tone and mood of the scene.

2. Time and Atmosphere: Specify the time of day and the weather conditions. These elements can enhance the mood—whether it’s a sunny, seemingly peaceful morning or a foggy, eerie evening.

3. Character Discovering the Body: Who finds the body? What is their relationship to the victim or the community? The character’s personality and initial reaction can add depth to the scene.

4. Victim Information: Details about the victim, such as their role in the community, their personality, and any known conflicts or relationships that might be pertinent to the plot.

5. State of the Crime Scene: Describe how the body is positioned, any signs of struggle, or unusual items or symbols present. This helps in setting up initial clues and red herrings.

6. Initial Clues: Mention if there are any immediate clues visible that may start to point the sleuth (and readers) in specific directions—like a dropped item, a footprint, or a piece of jewelry.

7. Sleuth’s Role: How does the sleuth come into the picture? Are they stumbling upon the scene, called to it, or do they hear about it from someone else?

8. Tone and Style Preferences: Since cozy mysteries typically avoid graphic descriptions, let me know how you prefer to handle the discovery. Should it lean more towards the emotional impact and mystery, or should it be more factual and detached?

Providing these details will help tailor the scene to fit seamlessly into your story’s narrative and style, enhancing both the setting and the plot’s unfolding.

</Response>

This is a good start, but I notice no scene beats. Of course, the AI doesn’t know the beats, so this is material you have to supply; after all, it’s your story. If I were writing the scene (someone tripping over a body), I would add these beats, starting from the inciting incident and then walking through the progression of complications (perhaps spilling their coffee and ruining the forensics or panicking and pulling the fire alarm).

Think of these scene briefs as pseudo-code, a guide using natural language to describe what you’re trying to do, what happens here, what happens there, and what it means to the protagonist in a structured way that presents it to the LLM.

Here is an example of a scene plan I used for my book, Not My Barista, ⁠1 a romantic comedy about an AI billionaire trying to get a romance writer to unknowingly fine tune his LLM to write like a human.

[Scene Plan]

Setting:

Heidi’s garage has become a makeshift band rehearsal space. The atmosphere is charged with the creative energy of the Girl Crew, and various musical instruments are scattered around. The walls are adorned with posters of rock legends, and the air is filled with the scent of creativity and passion.

Characters:

Henry: Secret billionaire, posing as a barista, struggling with his growing feelings for Gina.

Gina: NY Times bestselling author, trying out as the lead singer for the Girl Crew.

Ichiro “Itch the Pitch”: Internet influencer and Heidi’s father, known for his keen eye for fashion and branding.

Heidi, Wanda, Dixie, Anita: Members of the Girl Crew.

Inciting Incident:

Henry observes Gina deeply engrossed in playing ProsePolish, his AI-driven writing game, and is impressed by her high scores and the admiration she’s receiving from other players on the leaderboard.

Progressive Complications for Henry:

Complimenting Gina: Henry compliments Gina on her impressive scores on ProsePolish, but internally, he’s wrestling with the guilt of using her talent to enhance his AI, ProsePantry.

Interaction with Itch the Pitch: Ichiro offers Gina fashion advice, suggesting a more flamboyant style to match her emerging rockstar persona. Henry notices Gina’s discomfort and wonders if he should intervene.

Observing Gina’s Authenticity: Henry is captivated by Gina’s authenticity and raw talent, which only deepens his internal conflict about deceiving her with his true identity.

Jealousy: Seeing Gina interact closely with other band members and Itch makes Henry feel a twinge of jealousy, complicating his emotions further.

Crisis for Henry (Two Bad Choices):

Henry must decide whether to come clean about his identity and risk losing Gina’s trust or continue hiding his true self, which feels increasingly dishonest, especially as he watches her pour her heart into the game he created.

Decision Point:

Henry chooses to maintain his secret identity, rationalizing that revealing the truth at this moment could jeopardize their burgeoning relationship and the success of ProsePolish.

Climax:

Gina, excited about her leaderboard ranking, shares her joy with Henry, unaware of his inner turmoil. This moment heightens Henry’s internal conflict as he’s torn between his growing affection for Gina and the deception he’s perpetuating.

Scene Development:

Henry tries to engage in light-hearted banter with Gina, complimenting her on both her singing and her writing skills. However, his mind is clouded with guilt and uncertainty. As Gina interacts with Itch, discussing fashion and branding, Henry feels out of place and contemplates the complexities of their relationship. The scene reaches its peak when Gina, elated by her success in ProsePolish, seeks Henry’s approval, not knowing the truth behind his involvement in the game.

Henry leaves the garage with mixed feelings. He’s more enchanted by Gina than ever, but the weight of his secret looms larger. The scene ends with Henry making a silent promise to himself to find the right time to reveal his true identity, hoping it will not cost him Gina’s affection.

[/Scene Plan]

There are so many scene types that I can’t possibly cover them all. My friend J. Thorn has an excellent book called The Scene Archetype Handbook with ChatGPT Prompts, which includes seventy-four different scene archetypes, information to create the megaprompt for the scene brief, and genre-specific examples using the Conflict, Choice, and Consequence framework. He gives you the prompt to inform the AI about the type of scene it is writing and fills in the details before asking ChatGPT to write the scene.

The folks at the Future Fiction Academy have a video showing how to use Rexy Word Banger to sequence prompts. In this video, Elizabeth Ann West demonstrates how to write the scene brief, then write the scene, and then write the next scene. Because what was written is in the Big Bang Box, narrative logic consistency is maintained since the Big Bang Box is passed into the AI’s context window. The video is called: “Master Scene Briefs for Better Stories with AI” (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=SCsOvSG4Xc0) ⁠2

​[ ​32​ ]​

NARRATIVE CONTINUITY

Since a story is filled with cause-and-effect events, maintaining narrative continuity is crucial because it ensures that the plot progresses logically from one scene to the next. We human storytellers do it naturally because the story is in our minds. An AI assistant, however, has a more difficult time.

First, it has a limited context window, but more than that, it doesn’t intuitively know the relative importance of the material presented to it. For example, a small detail, such as a smoking gun, means a lot in a mystery, but if it was only mentioned once, the AI assistant, especially one who relies on RAG, might have forgotten all about it.

Therefore, it is up to us to provide the necessary context and guidance to help the AI maintain continuity as it writes scene after scene.

One way to maintain narrative continuity is to include a short summary of the previous scene in the scene brief. However, this method can sometimes lead to the AI repeating information or becoming confused, as it interprets the summary and the current scene as closely related.

A more effective solution is a Prompt Sequence, which allows you to feed the AI a series of prompts that build upon each other while maintaining the necessary context. By sequencing your prompts, you can guide the AI through the story’s progression and ensure that each scene follows logically from the previous one without the risk of repetition or confusion.

Remember that prompt sequencing includes the output of a previous prompt as input to a subsequent prompt.

While prompt sequencing in a chatbot, like ChatGPT and Claude Chat, is possible, the most significant disadvantage is “dirty data” inside the context window and the fact that you don’t know how far back the chatbot’s context window extends.

Let’s say you wrote the first scene with a chatbot, but you didn’t like everything it wrote. You need to change the critical details before writing the second scene. Otherwise, the chatbot will believe that those events you didn’t like are part of the story.

However, even if you change the events and put it back in the chat window, the original scene it wrote could still be inside the context window. You might observe the chatbot stubbornly sticking to the point that you didn’t want to be made. You can see this in the example in Chapter 44: Story Development Using ChatGPT. ChatGPT really wanted the villain to be corporate developers or outsiders but that takes away from the family feud. It was a struggle to steer ChatGPT away from the idea of land developers out to get both families.

Anyway, I digress. If I were really writing that story, I would have worked on the scene briefs in more detail and foreshadowed the villain at the town hall meeting. That said, I cannot tell the AI about any foreshadowing because, just like a kid without filters, the LLM will tell what it knows.

Hence, we are responsible for giving the AI writing assistant enough details of what happened before so it can build from them and hide information from it until the time is right.

MAINTAINING CONTINUITY WITH A CHATBOT

If you want to write scenes with a chatbot interface, the best thing to do is to start another conversation when you have changed things so that your context window is clean. Here are the steps.

1. Upload background information, such as Story Information, with everything clearly labeled in tags.

2. Upload a relevant summary of previous events and label it [previous scene], [story so far], or [background]. Don’t clutter this megaprompt with everything that happened, but only what you need the AI to know to write the current scene. This is especially true if you’re working on a mystery or suspense thriller. For example, if you had a previous scene from the murderer’s point of view and you’re writing the detective, you want to hide information from the detective. In that case, you should only include information the detective knows in the [story so far] section.

Some people wonder if they should include the synopsis or the outline in the story information. I personally don’t, but then I don’t really outline either. Since I’m a discovery writer, I decide ad hoc what to do with each scene. However, even if you do outline, you might only include the outline of what has already happened. If you include everything, including the happy ending, some LLMs will rush ahead and finish your story in a snap. The AI assistant tends to optimize, and therefore, it doesn’t see the reason for not taking what it knows about the ending and finishing the story in the first scene.

3. Prompt the chatbot like this.

<Prompt>

I’m going to give you background information to help you write a scene in my novel. Please read and understand the [Story Information] and the [Story so far] and keep it in mind when you write, but do not write anything until I tell you to. {upload or copy and paste in your story information and story so far}.

</Prompt>

Now that you have the information uploaded or copied into the chat, you can give your prompt to write the scene using the following [Scene Brief]. Words closer to the end of the prompt are more relevant to the LLM, which is why you put the Scene Brief last. It focuses on the LLM and tells it, “THIS” is what I want you to write.

Your prompt might look like this.

<Prompt>

Using the [Story Information] as background material, write the scene described in the following [Scene Brief] with narrative logic consistent with [Story So Far]. {copy and paste in the scene brief}

</Prompt>

MAINTAINING CONTINUITY WITH PLAYGROUND

One advantage of using a Playground or developer’s interface is the ability to use a System Prompt to keep background information. Another is the control of the context window, with the ability to edit the AI’s output, delete both user and assistant messages, and prune the context window.

These advanced techniques were discussed in Part IV. Advanced LLM Techniques as well as in the Future Fiction Academy video series: “How to Prompt AI For Fiction Writing!” (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bN1V9pc5TQA) ⁠1

Use a large-context window LLM. Adjust the parameters and dial up the max-length to get a decent response size.

1. Place [Story Information] in the System Box as background information for the LLM.

2. Write the following in the User Box:

Please continue the story we are writing with narrative logic consistent with what happened before {paste in summary of story so far}. Now, write the scene given in this scene brief {paste in the scene brief}.

3. Read what the AI wrote in the Assistant box and EDIT it.

4. Go back and delete the contents of the previous User Box, but leave the edited Assistant box in place, labeling it [previous scene].

5. Open up a User message BELOW the Assistant box and prompt it for the next scene.

Please continue the story we are writing with narrative logic consistent with what happened before (see above). Now, write the scene given in this scene brief {paste in the scene brief}.

Do you see what we’re doing here? We’re trimming the contents of the context window as we go so we don’t confuse the AI with irrelevant previous information. This is impossible to do with a Chatbot unless we open a new chat window, but then we have to copy and paste in the [Story Information], [Writing Style], etc.

As the story gets longer, you can delete the earlier User and Assistant messages and keep only the recent ones that are relevant to the scene you’re working on. Meanwhile, your story information and writing style stay in the System box, eliminating the need to “remind” the Chatbot every so often.

This is a benefit of using the Playground or a sequencing tool like Rexy.

Review this video where Steph Pajonas puts in two previous chapters to maintain narrative logic consistency. She uses Claude Chat to write, and she does it step by step, going through each scene beat. She doesn’t use Scene Briefs here but demonstrates narrative continuity by priming the pump with what happened before. “AI + MegaPrompts = Fiction MAGIC!” (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZCyQkzKZ9Jo) ⁠2
