Arthur is the mastermind behind the murder Penny witnessed: He committed the crime to silence a person who had uncovered his secret illegal activities, perhaps involving embezzlement or art forgery.

Arthur is obsessed with Penny: He sees her as his perfect companion, intelligent and unassuming, and plans to manipulate her into becoming his accomplice or even his captive.

Arthur uses his wealth and influence to control the investigation: He donates generously to the police department, putting pressure on Jack’s superiors to dismiss his suspicions.

Arthur gaslights Penny: He subtly manipulates her perception of reality, making her doubt her own instincts and question Jack’s motives.

This setup creates a complex and suspenseful dynamic, as Jack struggles to protect Penny from a villain who has gained her trust and appears harmless. Penny’s inability to see through Arthur’s facade adds to the tension and humor, as she misinterprets his actions and intentions, leading to near misses and escalating danger.

User:

Combining my ideas with the Investigation plot type described in Dangerous Love, please write an outline for humorous romantic suspense and include all seven of the romance plot points, with the seven suspense plot points. Make sure to label the plot points where they occur and follow all romance conventions. Make sure the villain’s character arc is also shown.

Model:

Humorous Romantic Suspense Outline:

Setup:

Hook + Death Grip: Penny Parsons, a quirky and meticulous librarian, discovers a dead body in the rare books section of the library, setting the stage for a murder investigation and disrupting her orderly world.

Cute Meet + Enter the Villain: Detective Jack Callahan, a rough-around-the-edges and impulsive investigator, arrives at the crime scene and immediately clashes with Penny, finding her attention to detail and adherence to rules frustrating. Arthur Blackwood, a charming philanthropist and major library donor, expresses concern for Penny and offers his support, subtly positioning himself as a friend and confidante.

Rising Action:

Locked and Loaded + Terrible Trouble: Penny becomes a key witness in the case, forcing her to interact with the infuriating Detective Callahan as they investigate the murder. Jack suspects Arthur of being involved but lacks evidence. Arthur, meanwhile, manipulates Penny with his charm and generosity, gaining her trust.

Oops, I’m Falling in Love + I Bought a Clue: Despite their constant bickering and contrasting personalities, Penny and Jack develop a grudging respect for each other, recognizing each other’s strengths during the investigation. Penny stumbles upon a clue, perhaps a rare book with a hidden inscription or a cryptic note, that points to Arthur’s involvement in the murder and his potential illegal activities.

Reminder of Danger: Arthur, sensing Penny’s growing suspicion, steps up his manipulation tactics, gaslighting her and subtly threatening her safety. He also plants false clues to mislead the investigation and divert attention away from himself.

Midpoint:

The Big Bad + All Is Lost: Penny and Jack have a heated argument, their conflicting approaches to the investigation coming to a head. Penny, influenced by Arthur’s manipulations, doubts Jack’s suspicions and accuses him of being paranoid. Jack, frustrated by Penny’s blind trust in Arthur, feels helpless to protect her. At the same time, Arthur’s illegal activities escalate, and he becomes more desperate to silence Penny before she uncovers the truth.

Falling Action:

Romantic Interlude: After a near-miss with danger, orchestrated by Arthur, Penny realizes her mistake and apologizes to Jack. They share a moment of vulnerability, acknowledging their growing attraction and the possibility of a romantic connection.

Danger to the Dark Moment: Arthur, feeling cornered, takes drastic measures. He kidnaps Penny, intending to silence her permanently or force her to become his accomplice. Jack, realizing Penny is in grave danger, races against time to find her, using the clues she uncovered and his own investigative skills.

Climax:

Grand Romantic Moment + Boss Battle: Jack confronts Arthur in a thrilling showdown, rescuing Penny and exposing his crimes. In the midst of the chaos, Penny and Jack declare their love for each other, recognizing the bond that has formed between them. Arthur, defeated and exposed, attempts to escape but is apprehended by the authorities.

Resolution:

Happily Ever After + Winner Winner: With the case solved and the villain apprehended, Penny and Jack can finally explore their relationship. Penny overcomes her initial reservations about his impulsive nature, while Jack learns to appreciate her methodical approach. They embrace their happily ever after, finding a balance between their contrasting personalities and enjoying their shared love of solving mysteries. Arthur faces the consequences of his actions, his carefully constructed facade of respectability shattered as he is brought to justice.

Villain’s Character Arc: Arthur Blackwood starts as a seemingly benevolent figure but is gradually revealed to be a cunning and manipulative criminal. His downfall is driven by his obsession with Penny and his desperation to maintain his facade of respectability.

You can also analyze and critique a work in progress against the reference materials.

Example Prompts:

“Analyze my story ideas and suggest a plot type from Dangerous Love that best fits my story.”

“I’d like to blend an additional plot type into my work in progress. Can you suggest additional plot points where we can introduce a Pursuit? List the specific chapters where these points will fit in.”

“List the types of villains in Dangerous Love. Design a villain that will best foil my hero, who is an overprotective macho man.”

“I’m writing a dark romantic suspense with a psychopath hero. Combining my ideas with the Disturbed plot type described in Dangerous Love, please write a step-by-step synopsis of the story.”

I’m giving readers who legally obtain DRM-free versions of my romance-writing ebooks permission to upload them to an AI system and use them to develop their stories. Romance in a Month, 366 Ways to Know Your Character, Love Stories: Writing a Romance Novella in 30 Days, Writing Asian Romance Characters, Dangerous Love: Writing a Romantic Suspense. ⁠3 Non-DRM EPUBs are available at Smashwords.com.⁠4 You can use a tool like Calibre ⁠5 to convert the EPUB to PDF.

MYSTERY

Upload how-to-write cozy mystery notes, images of the setting, and descriptions of crafts, cooking, or hobbies.

“Based on the characteristics of a cozy mystery in {document}, suggest a closed environment and a close-knit group of likely suspects. Make each suspect memorable.”

“Analyze the alibis of all the suspects and identify any inconsistencies or potential weaknesses.”

“Using the description of {crafts or hobbies}, list all the clues that point to each suspect and assess their relative strength and significance.”

“Review the detective’s current theory of the crime and identify any information from {knowledge of craft} that contradicts or challenges it.”

“Generate a list of potential red herrings {setting, crafts, recipes} that could be incorporated into the story to mislead the reader.”

“Examine the {uploaded photograph} of the quaint village bookshop. Describe its atmosphere and the types of books it might hold. Brainstorm hidden clues that might relate to the titles in the store.”

HORROR

Upload information on writing horror, documents of creepy places, monster descriptions and images.

“Combine my work in progress with the horror tropes in {document} and select three that fit best.”

“Analyze the protagonist’s fears and vulnerabilities, and suggest ways to exploit them to create suspense and horror.”

“Explore the history and mythology of the story world as described in {document}. Now, create the primary location of the haunted house and identify potential sources of supernatural threats.”

“Describe the physical characteristics and powers of the monster/antagonist, and suggest ways to make it more terrifying.”

“Analyze the use of foreshadowing and suspense throughout the story, and suggest ways to enhance the overall atmosphere of dread.”

“Examine {the uploaded image} of the abandoned asylum. Describe the atmosphere and emotions it evokes. How can this setting be used to enhance the horror elements of the story?”

ACTION

Upload videos of fight scenes. Timelines of action stories. Geography description, weather events, weaponry, etc.

“Write a fight scene of the style shown in {name of video} between my hero and villain. Describe it blow-by-blow using exciting language and sensory details.”

“Utilizing the weaponry described in {document}, add detail into the scene in Chapter 5.”

“Analyze the protagonist’s skills and motivations, and suggest ways to challenge them with increasingly difficult obstacles of the type described in {document}.”

“Evaluate the pacing and flow of the action scenes based on {timeline}. Identify places where they lag and suggest ways to increase the tension and excitement.”

“Describe the pursuit through {geography} and include challenges brought on by the terrain and weather. Create obstacles to escalate the stakes for the hero.”

FANTASY

Upload your world-building research, magic system descriptions and rules, cultures you invent, myths, and mythological creatures. Include AI-generated images to describe.

“Analyze my story based on the magic system of the world in {document}, including its rules, limitations, and potential consequences for its users. Point out contradictions or places where the magic use is unclear.”

“Explore the history and culture of the different races and factions within the world in {document}, and identify potential sources of conflict and alliances.”

“Analyze the protagonist’s journey and their role in the overarching prophecy/quest, and suggest ways to make their arc more compelling.”

“Examine the map of the fantasy kingdom {image uploaded}. Describe the different regions and their significance to the story. Create a dangerous trek for the protagonist and the obstacles he encounters as well as likely allies and discoveries along the way.”

COMING-OF-AGE

Upload character backstory, family history details, and psychology articles.

“Based on {document}, analyze the protagonist’s internal conflicts and emotional challenges, and suggest ways to depict their journey of self-discovery and growth.”

“Explore the relationships between the protagonist and their family, friends, and mentors, and identify key moments of influence and conflict. Using {document}, point out any pathological relationships.”

“Describe the social and cultural environment in which the story takes place based on {document of culture}, and suggest ways to use the setting to highlight the challenges and opportunities faced by the protagonist.”

“Analyze the protagonist’s transition from childhood to adulthood, and identify key turning points and moments of realization.”

SCIENCE FICTION

Upload documents on technology, such as quantum computing, nuclear fusion, etc. Using AI, generate images of settings, alien species, weapons, and clothing.

“Using the information in {document}, describe the technology and scientific advancements present in the story, and explore their impact on society and the characters.”

“Explore the ethical and philosophical implications of the scientific concepts presented in the story (such as artificial intelligence, genetic engineering, or space exploration) against the details in {document}.”

“Emulating the culture in {document}, describe the alien species or futuristic societies encountered by the protagonist, and analyze their culture, motivations, and interactions with humans.”

“Based on the physics in {document} evaluate the plausibility and internal consistency of the scientific concepts presented in the story.”

Generate an image with Midjourney or Dall-E and upload it. “Analyze the image of the futuristic cityscape. Describe the architectural style, technological advancements, and overall atmosphere. How does this setting reflect the themes and conflicts of the story?”

CRIME

Upload articles about criminal organizations. Upload crime scene photos and true crime narratives.

“Using information from {document} describe the criminal organization or individual responsible for the crime, including their motivations, methods, and ultimate goals.”

“Analyze the evidence and clues gathered by the investigators and suggest possible interpretations or leads to follow.”

“Explore the social and economic factors that contribute to crime in the story’s setting as described in {document}.”

“Describe the impact of the crime on the victims and the community, and explore themes of justice, revenge, and redemption.”

“Examine the crime scene photo uploaded. Describe the details of the scene and any potential clues that might be relevant to the investigation. Write a police report based on this photo.”

HISTORICAL FICTION

Upload historical documents, historical images, and timelines.

“By looking at the information in {document}, describe a village in detail and describe the townsfolk and their occupations.”

“Describe the heroine’s clothing based on {image}. Specify characteristics that signify her social class as described in {document}.”

“Write a scene showing our characters going through the storming of the Bastille as described in {document}. Note details from {document} and weave them into the scene.”

​[ ​PART 8​ ]​

FUTURE-PROOFING YOUR WRITING WITH AI

​[ ​41​ ]​

EMBRACING CHANGE AND COLLABORATION

One thing is clear. The world of AI is changing faster than anyone can keep up. Improvements and new technologies seemingly drop every day and the pace is accelerating. So how does an author who uses AI to assist his writing keep up?

The long answer is you can’t—not by yourself. However there are ways to embrace change, remain adaptable, but not spend all your time racing one LLM to another.

KEEP ON WRITING

It’s tempting to race from one new application or LLM to another, but remember you’re a writer first and an AI expert second. The best way to keep up is to join an AI writing community that is forward-thinking and adventurous. Realize that new AI models, tools, and techniques are constantly being developed. While it’s good to have one eye peeled on the latest and the greatest, there’s also a learning curve involved every time you swap tools and methods.

Stick with your writing process and insert AI where it will make the most difference. I’ve thrown a lot at you in this book, but don’t feel like you have to experiment with everything. Use the 80-20 rule and use a technique that can make the most impact on your writing process.

Remember, embracing AI doesn’t mean abandoning your traditional writing skills. Instead, view AI as an opportunity to enhance your existing process. Be ready to adapt when something compelling comes along, like Gemini 1.5 Pro’s ability to keep your entire manuscript in its context memory. But don’t jump on the latest tool of the day or fall for hype email and exploding countdown timers that take your focus off your craft and stories.

COLLABORATION AND COMMUNITY

There’s strength in numbers, and many eyes can exponentially increase everyone’s knowledge when people share. Join an AI writing community that is a supportive space where authors can connect, share experiences, and learn from one another. Don’t fall for email marketing or insane promises of one-click books with an expiring time bomb if you sign up in the next 37 seconds. Follow authors who have actual backlists and credibility outside of AI. As with any new thing, there are fly-by-nights popping up offering services, coaching, and secret systems preying on fear of missing out.

When I started my AI journey, I followed Elizabeth Ann West because I already knew her as an established indie author. There were other tools and courses out there, but I took Elizabeth’s Gumroad class. Her emphasis is always on the learner to understand what’s behind the prompting. She’s big on the need for transparency between the AI user and the LLM. This decreases frustration when you can’t understand how to modify a prompt because you can’t see what the LLM actually got. By providing a tool like Rexy, where users can see everything that goes into the LLM and what comes out, she has advanced the skills of writers using AI to include experimentation and adjustment of their prompts and fine-tuning to fit their personal writing process better.

Elizabeth and the rest of the Future Fiction Academy generously post informative videos on YouTube ⁠1 for free and are transparent about what works and what doesn’t. Join the Future Fiction Academy ⁠2 if you want up-to-date AI writing discoveries presented to you in interactive labs, structured classes, plus writing clubs and activities.

I also joined the AI Writing for Authors Facebook Group ⁠3, where I met Steph Pajonas, the group’s founder and one of the founders of the Future Fiction Academy. The group is a positive place for writers interested in how artificial intelligence can be used for writing. Thanks to the hard work of the moderators and admins, it has a supportive and sharing culture.

Whatever group you join, collaboration is the key, as is a willingness to be nonjudgmental, share, and include authors of different abilities, strengths, and outlooks.

Sharing your knowledge and experiences with others will not only benefit the community but also help you grow as an author and stay ahead of the curve.

BEYOND THE PAGE: EXPLORING NEW FORMATS

AI has the potential to revolutionize storytelling by enabling new and innovative formats that go beyond the traditional written word. Interactive narratives, where readers can influence the story’s direction, offer a more engaging and immersive experience. Videos and personal moviemaking are on the horizon. Personalized stories, tailored to individual reader preferences, can create a deeper connection between the author and the audience. Immersive experiences, combining text with virtual reality or augmented reality, can transport readers to entirely new worlds. Don’t be afraid to dream big and embrace the unexpected. You have the opportunity to be at the forefront of this creative revolution. Experiment with these emerging formats, push the boundaries of storytelling and discover new ways to connect with your readers, viewers, gamers, or collaborators.

ETHICS AND RESPONSIBILITY

As we embrace the power of AI, it’s crucial to use this technology responsibly and ethically. Concerns about bias, plagiarism, and the impact on human creativity are valid and require careful consideration. Always ensure that you have the right to use the data you provide to AI models, and be transparent about your use of AI if you are a service provider, such as a cover artist, ghostwriter, or editor.

THE FUTURE OF STORYTELLING

The future of AI and storytelling is full of possibilities. As AI models become more sophisticated and our understanding of human creativity deepens, we can expect to see even more innovative tools and techniques emerge. AI might even develop the ability to generate truly original and creative narratives, blurring the lines between human and machine authorship.

However, one thing remains certain: the story that comes from your heart is still yours. It doesn’t matter if billions of other people or LLMs tell their stories. Your emotions, experiences, and unique perspectives are what make your stories unique. No one can replace you as long as you remain authentic. It’s an exciting time that promises an ongoing adventure, exploration, journeys, and companionship along the way—just like a story—the story of you.

​[ ​42​ ]​

AUTHOR INTERVIEWS

STEPH PAJONAS, FUTURE FICTION ACADEMY

Steph Pajonas is a USA Today Bestselling author with over thirty books published in multiple genres, including cozy mystery and science fiction romance. She is also the founder of the Facebook Group “AI Writing for Authors” ⁠1 and the Future Fiction Academy (FFA). ⁠2

Q: What inspired you to create the Future Fiction Academy?

A: We decided to start the Academy because we saw that the most significant barrier to entry for authors into the world of AI was education. People needed to learn what these AI/LLMs were and how to use them, and Elizabeth, our other founding members, and I felt that we had enough real-world experience with AI to teach others how to use it, too.

Q: How has the FFA evolved since its inception?

A: In the beginning, we felt that we had to cover every single AI subject in order for authors to learn this, but we soon realized that authors needed to start right away and move through the subject matter slowly. We adapted our teaching style to provide smaller segments with actionable tasks so authors could learn by doing. We also realized that the FFA Community we built was a key part of the appeal to authors. They wanted a place where they could talk about these tools, learn them, and not be ostracized for trying them. I’m glad we’ve been able to provide that for them!

Q: What are some of the biggest challenges you’ve faced in building and growing the FFA community?

A: Our biggest challenge has definitely been serving a wide array of learning styles. We want to make this subject matter accessible for everyone, and I think we’ve come close! But in the beginning, it was tough figuring out how to design our curriculum so that it served as many people as possible.

Q: What are you most proud of in terms of the FFA’s accomplishments and impact on the writing community?

A: I’m super proud when I hear from students that they’ve learned enough skills to conquer their work. That we helped them break a lock or unlock a new level of skills that they didn’t have before. That really makes all the time and effort worth it.

Q: How do you see AI transforming the landscape of fiction writing in the next few years?

A: I think AI is going to enable new stories to enter the market from voices we haven’t been able to hear from yet. I’m very excited about the potential for new and exciting worlds that people will create with the aid of AI.

Q: What advice would you give to authors who are apprehensive about incorporating AI into their writing process?

A: What I always tell authors who are apprehensive but curious about adding AI to their writing process is to examine their process and determine a pain point that they always struggle with. Just one pain point. This is the best place to start. Then, use AI to help solve and reduce that pain point until they’re comfortable with the tools and what they can do. Once that pain point has been addressed, I encourage them to figure out the next natural place to add in AI, something that will give them either more free time or reduce the pain in their process. From there, each author figures out what works for them the best.

Q: What are some of the most popular and effective courses or workshops offered by the FFA?

A: I really like our new standalone classes that teach how AI works, how to prompt, and then how to add AI into each part of the writing process. We give authors highly actionable tasks and plenty of examples to get them going. https://futurefictionacademy.com/courses/ ⁠3

Q: How do you ensure that the FFA’s resources remain relevant and up-to-date with the rapid advancements in AI technology?

A: We meet as a team regularly and discuss new and exciting technology that’s emerging and could have an impact on our courses or our students. We subscribe to many newsletters, podcasts, and YouTube channels that help us stay on the bleeding edge. Anything we think that may be worth exploring, we do, and we always share our findings with our members.

Q: How do you envision the FFA community evolving and growing in the future?

A: I see the community as a place for authors who are serious about adding AI into their workflows to get the support they need. We want them to write the stories they’ve always wanted to write! And it’s our hope that the community thrives and becomes a place for them to find new information, friends, and colleagues.

Thanks, Steph. This is excellent advice, and I can attest that there’s nothing better than being in a vibrant and supportive community. AI is changing so fast that no one person can keep up, but the efforts of the FFA, along with its members, means that collectively, we can share our findings and experiments and help each other thrive as writers in this age of AI.

You can find out more about Steph and her writing at:

https://www.spajonas.com/ ⁠4 (Space Opera Romance)

https://www.stephgennaro.com/ ⁠5 (Cozy Mysteries in Japan)

LYNN JORDAN, INDEPENDENT AUTHOR

Q: How did you first discover the potential of AI for fiction writing?

A: As an IT professional, I understood how AI could be useful for nonfiction and images. I bought a lifetime subscription to one of the very early ones and used it to write articles and promotional material. I heard of people who had used it (ChatGPT) to write fiction and at least one product designed for fiction. I’d been away from writing fiction for publication for several years. My love for technology made me eager to check it out.

Q: What were your initial expectations and concerns about using AI in your creative process?

A: I didn’t really have any expectations. I wanted to see what it was all about. I also had several story ideas started or in my mind that I hadn’t touched in years.

Q: How has AI changed your approach to writing and storytelling?

A: I like to use AI similarly to how I used to use my critique group. It’s great for bouncing around ideas and tweaking plots, even at midnight. Critique partners are not always so accommodating.

Q: Which AI tools and techniques have you found most beneficial in your workflow?

A: I like MidJourney for making promotional images for my clients and myself. My main go-tos are Open AI’s ChatGPT models and Anthropic’s Claude models. For fiction, I like to use Sudowrite or Rexy (from Future Fiction Academy) to actually work on the story.

Q: Can you share an example of how AI has helped you overcome a specific writing challenge or achieve a desired outcome in your story?

A: AI helps me get out of plot holes much faster. As a pantser, I tend to go down blind alleys. AI can help me continue the story.

Q: How do you maintain your own voice and style while utilizing AI assistance?

A: Because I’ve been writing for so many years, I understand my own voice. I have no problem with changing anything the AI has generated to my voice.

Q: What are the biggest misconceptions you’ve encountered about AI-assisted writing?

A: There are quite a few misconceptions about AI from people who haven’t tried to use it, especially for fiction. First of all, the models are only computer programs. They aren’t plagiarizing anything. They have digested millions of books, so they know how some words naturally go with other words. I’ve read thousands of books, so I’m more likely to plagiarize something unconsciously from what I’ve read. A lot of the training material is older writing that is out of fashion today (public domain) or poorly written work that could be found with internet searches. Baseline AI writing is not great.

I have a lot of students who attend my writing with AI classes who have very mistaken expectations. No tool that uses AI can deliver a book with one button push. It takes a lot of effort and writing skill to get decent prose when assisted by AI. Using AI can help you write faster, but it isn’t ‘sit back and let the AI write a book.’ The AI models make most of the same mistakes that beginning writers make. The human senior partner needs to have the skill and knowledge to produce a book (or article) that will please readers.

Q: How do you envision the future of AI in fiction writing and storytelling?

A: I think AI will become an accepted writing tool such as typewriters, computers, spell and grammar checkers, and other writing aids.

Q: What advice would you give to authors who are curious about using AI but hesitant to take the first step?

A: Don’t be afraid to try it. Depending on your learning style, you can start with the online models and just ask questions. There are lots of free resources that will help you get started. YouTube is full of videos ranging from beginning lessons to advanced strategies.

Q: How has AI helped you with world-building, character development, or plot structuring within your chosen genre?

A: I write in several genres, so AI has really helped me with real-world things that I don’t know (which government agencies handle arms dealing), and also, things that I’m making up in my paranormal worlds.

Q: Are there any specific AI tools or techniques that you find particularly helpful for writing in your genre?

A: Because I’m such a techie, I’m trying to limit my tools without a lot of success. My writing tools are mainly ChatGPT and Claude, either directly or by using Sudowrite or Rexy as a front-end. MidJourney and DALL-E are great for images. (I know and use others, but I mostly use those.) I love AI voice products and use several of them to promote and edit videos. I also like to make pictures of characters and make them talk for promotions for my clients and me. My favorite tool for this is Studio.d-ID. I can waste far too much time playing (I mean diligently working) in there.

Q: How do you use AI to ensure your stories align with the expectations and conventions of your genre while also maintaining originality?

A: I make sure my work has my voice. I’m familiar with the conventions of the genres I write in. Sometimes, I have to keep reminding the LLMs of those conventions. The LLMs are good at providing some surprising twists, but this may be because they aren’t as familiar with genre conventions.

Q: Has AI helped you explore new possibilities or subvert traditional tropes within your genre?

A: The first book I completed with AI assistance was in a genre I had never written. I read in that genre but never thought to write it. Sudowrite needed a teacher for that genre, so I thought, why not? One book in the planning stage right now has to do with a motorcycle club. Never in my life would I have planned to write that.

Q: How has AI impacted your productivity and the speed of your writing process?

A: AI helps me write faster. As a pantser, I have thrown away a lot of prose. AI keeps me on track a little better, but I don’t mind tossing words since I can write faster now.

Q: Do you have any concerns about the ethical implications of using AI for writing?

A: No, I don’t. I’ve been around the publishing industry for more years than a lot of authors have been alive. I was teaching writers how to use computers to assist their writing when the first home computers became available (before IBM released their PC). At the time, some writers said they would never use a computer to write because it was cheating. Later, some of those same writers came to me to ask what word-processing program I used. I was around when Indie and small presses upended traditional publishing. AI is the next step in the evolution of writing tools.

Q: How do you see the role of AI evolving in the future of your writing career?

A: AI is helping me get back into fiction writing. I had given that up for non-fiction and promoting other people’s novels. AI has made writing fun again.

That’s for sure! And having fun is the best way to write or do anything in life. Thanks, Lynn, for sharing your insights with us.

You can learn more about Lynn on her YouTube channel. (https://www.youtube.com/@LynnJordan) ⁠6

KEYLA DAMAER, INDEPENDENT AUTHOR

My pen name is Keyla Damaer. I mainly write dystopian sci-fi, but I have recently published sci-fi horror stories. Thanks to AI, I also ventured into fantasy.

My dystopian sci-fi series is the Sehnsucht Series. Book 1 is available at all major retailers and my store https://shop.keyladamaer.com ⁠7

You can find everything else about me at https://keyladamaer.com ⁠8

Q: How did you first discover the potential of AI for fiction writing?

A: Last August, I needed to fix some minor developmental issues of the novel I’m about to publish, and I discovered how helpful AI could be in brainstorming. As artists, we often work by ourselves. That can be an asset but also detrimental when no one can answer our questions. AI is the companion I’ve always craved, and not it’s here.

Q: What were your initial expectations and concerns about using AI in your creative process?

A: Expectations, I had none. I didn’t think AI could be helpful until I started asking the right questions.

As for concerns, of course, I have them. Like all revolutions in technology, AI will be a blast stronger than the previous ones. People will lose jobs to it, even though new careers will be created. AI can be easily used for foul intentions, but that’s the case for many things in life: words, guns, and atoms. If people want to do harm, only the law and the right application of the law can stop them.

Q: How has AI changed your approach to writing and storytelling?

A: I’ve always been a slow writer. My first novel came out in 2020 in its second edition. I published the second and the third books in 2022 and 2023, but they were anthologies of short stories. My second novel will be out this year, and I’m already wiring the third one in the series. AI helps me with ideas and even with exposition, considering that English isn’t my first language. I don’t use it to translate chunks of prose, but it’s so helpful when I’m in doubt about things that can be lost in translation.

The bottom line is that I’m faster, and I can work on multiple projects at the same time, which is something I couldn’t do before.

A: Which AI tools and techniques have you found most beneficial in your workflow?

I used AI for audio, to create some covers of short stories, to edit, to brainstorm, and even to write. I still pay a human editor and a human artist for my major projects, but AI is just terrific. I can send cleaner drafts to my editor, and concepts of what I’d like to see on my covers to my cover artist.

Q: Can you share an example of how AI has helped you overcome a specific writing challenge or achieve a desired outcome in your story?

A: For the novel I will publish this year, I needed to write a fight scene between two soldiers and the aftermath. A knife was involved, and I needed to know the name of a specific part of the knife. With Google search, it would have taken me a lot of time to find what I needed. With AI, it was a matter of seconds. Of course, I checked it wasn’t lying to me, and that took me another bunch of seconds up to a minute. For some research, I used to spend weeks. Now, it’s a matter of minutes or hours if it’s something rather complex.

Q: How do you maintain your own voice and style while utilizing AI assistance?

A: I edit the crap out of the AI text. AI is repetitive and boring. Some people use fine-tuning, but I’m not at that level yet. I never had time to invest in learning that technique. It would probably save me time afterward, but for now, I’m procrastinating. If there were an easy guide on how to fine-tune, I’d probably get to it.

Q: What are the biggest misconceptions you’ve encountered about AI-assisted writing?

A: People tend to say AI can write a book by pushing a button, and this will ruin the market, but then they say the outcome is mediocre, to say the least. Either AI can create a book by pushing a button, or it can’t. And trust me, it can’t. Perhaps it will one day, but we’re far from there. Companies investing in AI are interested more in a scientific approach than in a literal one because AI could make life easier and better for all of us if used properly. Think about the health system and our health in general. I doubt investments in creating perfect prose are in the minds of many AI developers. It’s also a matter of money. Health pays back more than art. It’s sad, but it’s true.

Q: What advice would you give to authors who are curious about using AI but hesitant to take the first step?

A: Try it. AI is a tool like any other. It won’t bite you.

Q: How has AI helped you with world-building, character development, or plot structuring within your chosen genre?

A: I write sci-fi, and AI is obviously a blast with it. For my third book, I moved the crew of a ship to another galaxy. On the planet where the story takes place, there are aliens. Aside from needing a description of the creatures, I brainstormed with AI about their past and how to explain something in the present that I needed to be in a certain way. Together, we created a new species, of which I had only a vague idea at first.

Regarding plot structuring, I’ve always thought I was a plotter, but it turns out I’m more of a pantser, and that’s something that has slowed me down in my writing in the past.

Last autumn, I wanted to write a short story to distribute for free to my subscribers. For the first time, I wrote an outline and refined it with AI. We brainstormed every aspect of the story and then wrote it together.

Q: Are there any specific AI tools or techniques that you find particularly helpful for writing in your genre?

A: I’ve been told I’m a weaver, an advanced weaver, to be precise, which means I don’t care about the tool as long as it serves the purpose of a project. For economic reasons, I don’t intend to add another monthly subscription to my costs; therefore, I will stick to free AI to write and create art.

Since last August, I have used Chat GPT3, 3.5, and NovelCrafter during its trial window—I discovered OpenRouter thanks to it—Gemini Advanced only for the trial period, and now I’m using Gemini 1,5 pro because it’s free and it has a 1M token window. I have to activate a VPN to access it because it’s not available in my country. I have a feeling that Google is scared of GDPR because many of its services aren’t available in Europe. This scares me a bit, but Google already has all my files through my subscription to Google One, so what else can they get they don’t already have? The Big Brother has been here for many years.

I don’t use any specific technique. Like I said, I’d like to learn fine-tuning.

Q: How has AI impacted your productivity and the speed of your writing process?

A: Since last August, I concluded a novel, and started a new one. I published one short story, edited two short stories that needed dev editing, and wrote three short stories from zero. I’m faster, and beta readers didn’t notice a drop in quality. As an experiment, I didn’t disclose AI involvement to all beta readers. Comments were always positive.

Q: Do you have any concerns about the ethical implications of using AI for writing?

A: Of course I do. I believe the way AI was trained before it came out is immoral, and artists whose material was used should be compensated. But I, to be honest, I don’t have faith in the system. Artists won’t be paid because this would cost too much to companies like OpenAI, Microsoft, and Google. It’s not right, but AI is needed for the greater good, and no one will have the courage to damage these companies to compensate artists economically. It’s one of those cases where the needs of the many outweigh the needs of the few. I’m not saying it’s fair, but few things in life are. I hope to be wrong.

Q: How do you see the role of AI evolving in the future of your writing career?

A: I can see AI as my forever collaborator, and the only idea makes me smile wide.

Q: Will AI be able to replace all authors in the future?

A: That’s possible, but there’s nothing I can do to put the genie back in the bottle. It’s here, and I prefer to have it on my side.

CLARE CHU, INDEPENDENT AUTHOR

I am another pen name for Rachelle Ayala, so my answers will be for both Rachelle and Clare. While Rachelle started writing in 2010, well before ChatGPT, I used AI from the get-go to write zany and quirky books, such as the Misguided Guides series with titles like Why Your Cat is Plotting to Kill You (FREE)⁠9, Swiping Wrong: Online Dating for Zeroes⁠10, and Sit, Stay, Heel: Training Your Boyfriend Like a Pro⁠11.

You can find out about me at https://clarechu.com ⁠12 and Rachelle at https://rachelleayala.net ⁠13

Q: How did you first discover the potential of AI for fiction writing?

A: No one could miss the big splash ChatGPT made in late November, but Rachelle was on a deadline, so she ignored the news while writing over the holidays. Then, in January 2023, anti-AI authors made a big stink on one of the groups, and she got curious. She found Elizabeth Ann West’s Gumroad courses and charged ahead.

Q: What were your initial expectations and concerns about using AI in your creative process?

A: Neither of us had any. I’m a tech babe from the 1970s, and I’ve always been at the forefront in computing: parallel processing in the 80s, neural networks in the ‘90s, and internet, web-based network management, wireless, and virtual servers in the 2000s before retiring from tech and writing romances. It’s probably not wise to charge ahead before doing something, but my focus has always been, wouldn’t it be cool if we could … rather than should we …?

Q: How has AI changed your approach to writing and storytelling?

A: I added a brainstorming partner (actually many by now) who would chat with me any time of the day or night. I’m still focused on writing great stories and coming up with my own ideas. I’ve also discovered that I’m competitive with AI, so if they give me five lame ideas, I’ll go back with something they didn’t think of. Then they’re always so affirming and agreeing that my idea was better and list all the ways it can work.

Clare here. I also bounce things off them and ask for improvements, but I don’t take their suggestions as much because “I know better.”

Q: Which AI tools and techniques have you found most beneficial in your workflow?

A: Hands down, it’s Future Fiction Academy’s Rexy Word Banger. No other tool allows you to “program” your writing by sequencing prompts, using “variables” in the form of Notion slots, and including a smorgasbord of LLMs with the ability to tune parameters for each prompt. I generated Why Your Cat is Plotting to Kill You and published it in three hours start to finish. I brainstormed with Claude and ChatGPT 4. I used ChatGPT 4 to write the scene briefs, and then I plugged everything into Rexy Word Banger and generated the prose. I edited it and published it with a cover made with Midjourney. All this was done the night before I had to get on an airplane and I couldn’t sleep.

Q: Can you share an example of how AI has helped you overcome a specific writing challenge or achieve a desired outcome in your story?

A: Not really. I have to think my own way out of the dastardly blind alleys I get into that always seems to happen about two-thirds of the way. AI’s ideas usually give me what not to do, so that is helpful. The reason I don’t do what they suggest is that they tend to go in a straight line to the solution, which lacks conflict. I mean, if my heroine could just sit down and talk about it over a cup of tea, everything would be okay, right? Not.

Clare here. AI definitely helps me with my humor books because it gives me many undesired outcomes, and that works for my misguided guidebooks, which are not supposed to work. Cue evil laugh.

Q: How do you maintain your own voice and style while utilizing AI assistance?

A: I don’t worry about it because I edit everything. However, now that we have many LLM choices and the ability to fine-tune the GPT models, I generate several versions, pick and choose, and rewrite everything.

Q: What advice would you give to authors who are curious about using AI but hesitant to take the first step?

A: Get a free account and start chatting. AI is coming to you anyway, so you’ll see it popping up in your search engines, word processors, and grammar-checking tools. You’re already using AI without knowing about it. Wouldn’t it be better to see what it can do for you?

Q: How has AI helped you with world-building, character development, or plot structuring within your chosen genre?

A: Clare here. AI does great with short nonfiction that doesn’t have to be “correct.” That’s my humorous, misguided self-help books. We can go off on the craziest tangents. After all, when writing about time travel or supervillain success secrets, anything goes. I don’t have to fact-check, and I love it when AI hallucinates because exploring rabbit holes and going on wild goose chases are what my self-nonhelp genre is all about.

Rachelle: That was not helpful. She wants to know about storytelling not goof-off books. You’re supposed to say that AI can draw insights from its vast training and⁠—

Clare: Ahem, I’m the one being interviewed since I’m a pure-AI author??? You wrote 89 pre-AI books—that’s like pre-history if you ask me.

Q: Moving on … How has AI impacted your productivity and the speed of your writing process?

A: It has actually slowed down my book releases. In 2015, I wrote and published 14 books, in 2016, 12, but last year, with AI, I only had 9.

Clare: What are you talking about? I used AI and published 6 misguided guidebooks in a month.

Rachelle: Those are not serious writing and less than a hundred pages each. I have to worry about story, conflict, emotions, character arc … and facts, don’t forget facts and series consistency.

Clare: Hey. You took over MY interview. AI makes my genre possible, from inspiration to cover art. Not my fault if writing romances and AI writing books take too much time while you put me and my supervillain success secrets on the back burner …

Q: Ahem. Do you have any concerns about the ethical implications of using AI for writing?

A: Sure we do. Using AI in itself is not unethical just like using banks are not unethical. Uh, I believe you meant robbing banks is unethical. Yes, stop cutting in. I’m saying copying and pasting other people’s work into your prompt is no bueno. Agree. But what about all of those prompt books? You know what I’m talking about. Prompt book authors are selling prompts for you to copy and paste. I’m talking about people’s prose, even if it’s for analysis. If the author in question is sitting by you, would you do it? If not, then don’t do it.

What about all the stuff that was scraped off the internet? I think people post stuff on the internet because they wanted people to read their stuff. Doesn’t mean it isn’t copyrighted. Then it’s fair use. Just because AI is smarter than the average bear doesn’t mean it’s wrong. Sure, sure, but aren’t you the least bit scared that we might all turn out to be pets of our AI overlords someday? You know, begging for treats while everything they do goes over our heads? What does that have to do with scraping the internet? Unintended consequences. I’m sure those guys who donated sperm thirty years ago had no intention of being tracked down⁠—

Interviewer: Last question. How do you see the role of AI evolving in the future of your writing career?

Clare: What future? Rachelle’s hogging up my brain cycles when I could be dancing with Sexy Rexy. I’m going to plot revenge. Maybe a self-help guide on shutting up your other personality is due.

Rachelle: You know, for a pen name, you’re awfully annoying.

Clare: At least I have a social security number. You, my dear, are nothing but a figment of my imagination. Shall I pull the plug?

Interviewer: Thanks, ladies. That was … insightful. Do you have any AI writing samples you’d like to share?

Clare: Me, me, me! Please see Chapter 45: Scene from Sit, Stay, Heel: Training Your Boyfriend Like A Pro

AMY SIMONE, K.L. BORDEAUX, INDEPENDENT AUTHOR

Amy Simone is one pen name www.theuptownwriter.com ⁠14

K. L Bordeaux is my other multi-genre pen name.

At last count, I had 22 books published, five with AI. Prior to publishing those 22, I had written four others six months before my first published book. I did journalism for about 30 years before that.

Q: How did you first discover the potential of AI for fiction writing? A: Joanna Penn’s podcasts led me to Sudowrite in Nov. 2022.

Q: What were your initial expectations and concerns about using AI in your creative process?

A: It felt like a playground or candy store. I was so happy to realize that all things seemed possible. I felt much better once I realized that plagiarism was not really possible due to the randomness of the token/next word selection by the AI. I was informed about all that via the Future Fiction Academy in May 2023.

Q: How has AI changed your approach to writing and storytelling?

A: It’s made me much more focused on structure. I initially thought I’d be zipping through books (creating them faster), but no, in fact, it’s become the opposite in many ways.

Q: How do you maintain your own voice and style while utilizing AI assistance?

A: I use my own fine tunes PLUS copious editing of AI content. Sometimes, I have to toss about 75% of AI-generated material.

Q: What are the biggest misconceptions you’ve encountered about AI-assisted writing?

A: The claim that it is full of plagiarism. Not true.

Q: How do you envision the future of AI in fiction writing and storytelling?

A: More ease; more of my own voice carrying through. I would also like to see tools developed that escalate the conflict or show writers how to do that in their stories. Also, a purging of the circular nature (looping) that AI can sometimes do.

Q: What advice would you give to authors who are curious about using AI but hesitant to take the first step?

A: I know I COULD blithely say, “C’mon in, the water’s fine,” but that’s a disservice to folks who’ve never dabbled in using AI. I think if they just played with any of the chat features and winged it and asked any questions about it, no matter how wild, they’d be as delighted as I was.

Q: Are there any specific AI tools or techniques that you find particularly helpful for writing in your genre?

A: Role-playing: telling the AI to “act as if” it was so-and-so and how so-and-so would handle a certain situation/conflict.

Q: How do you see the role of AI evolving in the future of your writing career?

A: AI is making me a better storyteller, giving me the ability to set stories in places/ways I’d never dreamed of before. For example, I could so easily set a story on another planet and build an entire world in a matter of minutes using AI. Something like that might have taken me months pre-AI.

DANICA FAVORITE

Website: https://danicafavorite.com/ ⁠15

Q: How did you first discover the potential of AI for fiction writing?

A: I had seen authors talking about it online, and because I’d already been using it for marketing purposes, I was curious about the potential.

Q: What were your initial expectations and concerns about using AI in your creative process?

A: I wondered about copyright and legalities, and what was okay to use. I was also concerned about the quality of AI output. Because I had been using it for marketing copy, I knew it would require substantial edits, so I wasn’t sure if it would be a useful tool for fiction. Since then, I have taken the time to educate myself on all of these issues, and I feel very comfortable using AI in my writing process.

Q: How has AI changed your approach to writing and storytelling?

A: Absolutely! I think the authors who are afraid of AI taking over need to do some digging into using AI themselves, because the biggest thing I’ve learned is that I’m a great writer and storyteller. Without human intervention, AI can’t tell a good story. Analyzing the various AI outputs has taught me that I know a LOT about writing and what goes into a good story. In a lot of ways, it’s taught me more about story structure, because I’ve had to learn how to explain it, and what I need to do to better hit the mark. I’m now more aware of making sure my story structure is sound, and I think that is because of how much evaluating I’ve had to do.

Q: Which AI tools and techniques have you found most beneficial in your workflow?

A: I’m still a big fan of Chat GPT. I have played with other models, and I’m starting to really love Google’s Gemini, because of how seamlessly it has worked into my dictation process. Dictation has been my primary method for writing for at least seven years now, so using AI for transcription and transcription cleanup has been really important to me. Before Chat GPT, I used Dragon Dictation, which was okay, but required a lot of manual work on my part. Chat GPT was a great addition for cleanup, but now that Gemini is integrated right into my phone, it’s a total game changer and a seamless process. I also use Chat GPT daily for a lot of menial tasks, like writing marketing copy, brainstorming, etc.

Q: Can you share an example of how AI has helped you overcome a specific writing challenge or achieve a desired outcome in your story?

A: I tend to use a lot of repetition. Sometimes on purpose, sometimes on accident. I use AI a lot to look at where I’m repeating myself, and then to give suggestions on alternate ways I can word things. I no longer spend hours (literally! UGH!) staring at the computer screen, trying to figure out how I can reword a paragraph that uses the same phrase over and over. I pop it into Chat GPT, and ask for some options. Sometimes I don’t even like the options I’m given, but having a bunch of fresh ideas makes me think of what I need to do to make the piece better.

Q: How do you maintain your own voice and style while utilizing AI assistance?

A: I always start with my own writing. I LIKE my own writing, and I like the process of creating it. So, since I start there, I think keeping my own voice is a lot easier. And, because I AM a good writer, I can look at AI suggestions and analyze, okay, is this what I want? Does this still sound like me? In one example where I was teaching people about using AI, I showed them where the AI changes made my small town hero sound really highbrow. Because I understand the needs of my story, I knew it wasn’t going to work. And again, that’s where we as humans are always going to be needed in the creative process. AI doesn’t know that a small town cowboy isn’t going to use a lot of big fancy words. Unless, of course, that is true to the character, but in most cases, things like that are what you need to look for and change.

Q: What are the biggest misconceptions you’ve encountered about AI-assisted writing?

A: I could write a book on this! So could you! The biggest one is that it’s stealing other authors’ work. AI does not work that way. It’s not going to lift pages, or even lines, of text from someone else’s work, unless you force it to do that, just like if you copy from someone else’s book manually. What we call AI is really just predictive text like when you play those fill in the blank games. It’s slightly more sophisticated than that, but really, when you’re asking it to write a sentence or paragraph, all it’s doing is thumbing through its metaphorical dictionary and putting words that make sense together. The other misconception that I want people to understand is that using AI isn’t lazy and it doesn’t mean you don’t know how to write. I spoke about this a little bit before, but it bears repeating. If you don’t know how to write a compelling story, and what elements are needed for your genre, any AI output you get is not going to be good quality. There’s also a pretty steep learning curve to get AI to do the work you want it to do. Again, in order to make it do those things, it’s going to take a lot of effort on the part of the author. However, using AI can make you more efficient in the tasks you struggle with so that you can better utilize the writing time you have on the parts of writing you really love.

Q: How do you envision the future of AI in fiction writing and storytelling?

A: I think it’s going to become a natural part of most writers’ processes. It’s never going to replace the writer, but I think we’re going to see it being included more and more.

Q: What advice would you give to authors who are curious about using AI but hesitant to take the first step?

A: There is a learning curve, so be patient. I’ve seen a lot of authors smugly state that they tried to get Chat GPT to write something for them, and it was garbage, so AI is a passing fad. Um, no. They just didn’t know how to prompt it correctly to get the desired output. It takes time to learn the different models, and every model behaves differently. I mentioned that I use Chat GPT a lot, and in my opinion, it’s the easiest and most flexible for someone just starting out. So start there, learn the basics, and then you can see what works best with your process.

Q: Are there any specific AI tools or techniques that you find particularly helpful for writing in your genre?

A: The biggest technique that I find helpful for anything I write is to be willing to ask questions of the AI, and treat it like a conversation. If I’m not getting the results I want, then I ask it HOW to get the results I want or why it gave me those results. One of the things I’m loving about Gemini right now is that when I ask it to rewrite something for me, not only does it give me a rewrite, but it also tells me the logic it used to come up with that rewrite, as well as things I might think about when considering those changes.

Q: How do you use AI to ensure your stories align with the expectations and conventions of your genre while also maintaining originality?

A: Ask it to do the research. A year ago, I would not have made this suggestion, but with the advances in the LLM models, you can easily ask it about the expectations and conventions of the genre, and it will give you pretty good answers. Then you can feed it some of your stuff to see if it hits the mark. And then … hahaha … this is where your humanness needs to step in, you get to evaluate those responses to make sure they’re correct.

Q: Has AI helped you explore new possibilities or subvert traditional tropes within your genre?

A: I have not used it for this … yet. I am looking at writing under a new pen name for funsies, and it’s definitely a twisted trope. I have not been satisfied with the results it’s given, but I also have not tried very hard.

Q: How has AI impacted your productivity and the speed of your writing process?

A: That’s a double-edged sword. I’ve been LESS productive in the sense that I’m having so much fun playing that I’m not getting as much done. But, I also recognize that play time is learning time, so even though I don’t have published words to show for it, I AM moving forward because I understand how the LLMs work better. It has definitely made more more productive in writing marketing copy, both for my day job, and for the books I write, because I’m not staring at a blank screen for hours. And, it’s made my transcription process sooo much faster and more efficient.

Q: Do you have any concerns about the ethical implications of using AI for writing?

Yes and no. Yes, because I can see where people can abuse it and do things like plagiarize. Once again, I want to state that AI cannot plagiarize unless you explicitly ask it to. But that’s where I come to where I said I am not concerned about the ethical implications of using AI for writing. There are always going to be bad actors. If we focus all our attention on stopping the bad actors, we’ll never have time to focus on our writing. Instead, I want to focus on who is doing it well, and learning from them, so that we as a community are growing with AI and aren’t afraid to use it.

Q: How do you see the role of AI evolving in the future of your writing career?

A: It’s going to be a much more integrated part of my process. It’s already there, but I’m always looking at how and where I can improve.

​[ ​PART 9​ ]​

GLOSSARY AND SUPPLEMENTARY MATERIALS

​[ ​43​ ]​

GLOSSARY OF TERMS

AI (Artificial Intelligence): A broad field of computer science that encompasses the development of intelligent agents, which are systems that can reason, learn, and act autonomously.

AI-assisted writing: The use of AI tools and technologies to enhance the writing process, such as generating ideas, outlining plots, crafting dialogue, or editing and revising text.

ANTCONC: A software tool used for analyzing text and creating concordances, which show the frequency and context of words in a text.

API (Application Programming Interface): A set of protocols and tools that allows different software applications to communicate with each other. In the context of AI writing, APIs allow users to access and interact with large language models (LLMs) directly.

Anthropic: An AI research and safety company that developed the Claude family of LLMs.

AutoCrit: A fiction writing software that offers manuscript analysis, editing suggestions, and AI-powered tools for story development and refinement.

Bias: The tendency of AI models to reflect the biases present in their training data, leading to potentially unfair or discriminatory outputs.

Big Bang Box (Rexy): A feature in the Rexy prompt sequencing tool that stores the output of each step in a prompt sequence and allows it to be used as input for subsequent steps, ensuring narrative continuity.

Chatbot: A computer program designed to simulate conversation with human users, often using natural language processing and AI techniques.

Claude: A family of LLMs developed by Anthropic, known for their conversational abilities and creative writing style.

Completion Prompting: A prompting technique where the user provides a partial text and instructs the AI to continue or complete it.

Context stuffing: A prompting technique where the user provides additional information and context beyond the core prompt to guide the AI’s response.

Context Window: The number of tokens (words or word fragments) that an AI model can consider at a time when generating text.

Custom GPTs (ChatGPT Plus): A feature that allows users to create and train AI models tailored to specific domains, writing styles, or preferences.

Custom Instructions (ChatGPT): A feature that allows users to provide specific guidelines and information to influence the AI’s responses in a chat session.

Developmental Editing: A type of editing that focuses on the overall structure, plot, characters, and themes of a story, helping to ensure a cohesive and engaging narrative.

Diffusion Model: A type of deep learning model used for generating images from text prompts.

Fine-Tuning: The process of training an existing AI model on a smaller, more specific dataset to adapt its behavior or improve its performance on a particular task.

Foundational Model: A large language model trained on a massive dataset of text and code, designed to be adaptable to various downstream tasks through fine-tuning.

Future Fiction Academy (FFA): A community and educational platform for authors who are interested in using AI for fiction writing. Membership includes access to Rexy, a prompt sequencing tool.

Generative AI: A type of AI that can create new content, such as text, images, music, or code, that is similar to human-created content.

Genre Conventions: Established norms and expectations within a particular genre of fiction, such as character archetypes, plot structures, and thematic elements.

GPT (Generative Pre-trained Transformer): A family of large language models developed by OpenAI, known for their ability to generate human-quality text.

Grammarly: An AI-powered writing assistant that offers grammar and style checking, as well as suggestions for improving clarity and conciseness.

Hugging Face: A website that provides information about a vast collection of open-source AI models and datasets.

In-Context Learning: The ability of an AI model to learn and adapt to new information and tasks within a specific context, without requiring retraining on a large dataset.

Iterative Prompting: A prompting technique where the user refines and adjusts prompts based on the AI’s responses, gradually shaping the generated content to align with their vision.

JSONL (JSON Lines): A text file format where each line contains a JSON object, often used for storing and exchanging data between AI models and applications.

Large Language Model (LLM): A type of AI model trained on a massive dataset of text and code, capable of understanding and generating human-quality text, translating languages, writing different kinds of creative content, and answering your questions in an informative way.

Megaprompting: A prompting technique that involves providing extensive context and information within a prompt to guide the AI’s response and ensure accuracy and relevance.

Midjourney: An AI image generation tool that allows users to create images from text descriptions.

Mixture-of-Experts (MoE): A type of neural network architecture that uses a collection of specialized sub-models, or “experts,” to process different parts of the input data.

Multimodal: Refers to AI models that can process and generate information in multiple formats, such as text, images, audio, and video.

Narrative Continuity: The consistency and logical flow of events within a story, ensuring that plot points connect and characters’ actions and motivations align with past events.

Natural Language Processing (NLP): A branch of AI that focuses on enabling computers to understand, interpret, and generate human language.

Needs Assessment Prompting: A technique where the user asks the AI what information it needs to complete a given task effectively, leading to more tailored and effective prompts.

Novelcrafter: An AI storytelling platform that offers tools for generating text, creating characters, and developing storylines.

Notion: A flexible workspace and note-taking application that allows users to organize information, create databases, and collaborate on projects.

NSFW (Not Safe for Work): Refers to content that is considered inappropriate for viewing or sharing in a professional setting, often due to its explicit or offensive nature.

OpenAI: An AI research and deployment company that developed the GPT family of LLMs and other AI tools.

Open-Source: Refers to software or models that are made available to the public with a license that allows users to view, modify, and distribute the code.

Parameters: Numerical values that define the behavior and characteristics of an AI model, such as the size of its context window or the randomness of its outputs.

Persona: A specific role or character that is assigned to the AI to guide its responses and provide a framework for its interactions with the user.

Playground: A developer interface provided by many LLM platforms that allows users to experiment with prompts, adjust parameters, and explore the capabilities of the AI model.

Plot Threads: The different storylines or narrative arcs within a story that contribute to the overall plot development.

Prompt: A piece of text that serves as input or instructions to guide the LLM’s response. A prompt provides context, sets expectations, and directs the AI toward generating desired outputs, such as text, code, or images.

Prompt Chaining: A prompting technique that involves breaking down a complex prompt into a series of smaller, interconnected prompts to guide the AI through a logical thought process.

Prompt Engineering: The art and science of crafting effective prompts to elicit desired responses from AI models.

Prompt Sequencing: A technique that involves creating a sequence of prompts that build upon each other, allowing the AI to maintain narrative logic and consistency across generated text.

ProWritingAid: A grammar checker and style editor that also offers AI-powered suggestions for improving clarity, conciseness, and overall writing quality.

RAG (Retrieval Augmented Generation): A technique where AI models use external information retrieval systems to access and incorporate relevant information when generating text.

Rexy Word Banger: A prompt sequencing tool developed by the Future Fiction Academy, allowing users to create and customize sequences of prompts to guide AI models in generating text.

Role-Based Prompts: Prompts that assign a specific role or persona to the AI, guiding its responses and providing a framework for its interactions with the user.

Scene Brief: A detailed outline of a scene, including setting, characters, goals, conflicts, and key events, used to guide AI models in generating scene descriptions and dialogue.

Second Brain: A system or tool used to store and organize information, acting as an extension of the user’s memory and cognitive abilities.

Story Bible/Blueprint: A comprehensive document that contains all the essential information about a story or series, including character profiles, setting descriptions, plot outlines, and world-building details.

Sudowrite: An AI writing tool specifically designed for fiction writers, offering features for generating text, overcoming writer’s block, and refining prose.

System Prompt/Instruction: A set of instructions or guidelines provided to the AI model at the beginning of a session to influence its overall behavior and response style.

Temperature: A parameter that controls the randomness and creativity of an AI model’s output.

Token: The basic unit of text used by AI models, typically representing a word or part of a word.

XML (Extensible Markup Language): A markup language that defines a set of rules for encoding documents in a format that is both human-readable and machine-readable.

​[ ​44​ ]​

STORY DEVELOPMENT USING CHATGPT

Since ChatGPT is still the most popular AI tool for writers, I decided to use It to go from Ideation and Concept through Story Development all the way to an outline, scene briefs, and the writing of two scenes. I then tested the scene writing on various LLMs, including my fine-tune models, to show how the output can differ even with the same prompts. Furthermore, each run of the LLM is like a word slot machine, and you could get something quite different.

A year ago, I wrote Love by the Prompt ⁠1 using ChatGPT for the entire book. What’s interesting is that those techniques still work, although the possibilities for generating prose have expanded. You can go from ideation, brainstorming, character and setting development, to plot and outline using nothing but ChatGPT. You can also write skeleton scenes with ChatGPT, and that might be all you need.

So, if you have ChatGPT, you can follow along with the following conversation.

Link to the ChatGPT 4 conversation:

https://chat.openai.com/share/fb7c31f7-b813-43f7-93f1-cea044d4e8cd ⁠2

Initial prompt: You are a bestselling romance author for small-town romance looking to write an enemies-to-lovers romance involving a family feud and a missing prize racehorse. Make this a dramatic contemporary romance full of romantic tension and chemistry between the hero and heroine as well as a touch of suspense. Give me a high-concept premise for this romance.

I then continued the conversation, refining and changing direction from what ChatGPT 4 gave me. Notice you don’t have to take what it gives you. You can and should inject your own opinions.

I generated the hook and pitch, as well as a list of characters and settings. I then asked ChatGPT 4 to create scene briefs by giving it an overview of how I wanted the scene to go.

I can intervene and change things at every step of the process. Of course, this is a demo, so I kind of let it generate an outline that I wouldn’t use since I’m a pantser. The outline was very sketchy, so I suggested the first two scenes in alternating point of view between the heroine and hero, and then let ChatGPT generate the prose.

Once you get to the end of the conversation you’ll notice a few things.

ChatGPT 4 was pretty good at developing the characters, but you can see that heavy guidance was needed to make the story more dramatic. One of the problems with AI, especially chatbots that are meant to be “nice,” is that they tend to minimize conflict. If you read through the chat, you can see that the proposed story lacks conflict for a romance.

This is where you, as the human storyteller, need to come in and course-correct.

Unfortunately, even with heavy guidance for the scenes, the writing was not the best. These written scenes would have to be heavily edited or used for inspiration to write from.

Notice that the outline doesn’t make sense near the end. If Brooke and Jake find out about the true kidnapper in Chapter 15, who makes a confession about his plans to kill Brooke, then why is he still around in Chapter 16 to attempt a murder at an equestrian event?

Since I’m not a plotter and I don’t use outlines, I’m not going to bother to correct the outline. I’m also not going to write this story since I don’t know that much about horses and equestrian events, so I don’t know if I would have started at the town hall either. It would probably be better to have the meet-cute at an equestrian event where Brooke could accuse Jake of cheating or something equally obnoxious before her horse disappears.

Since ChatGPT 4’s writing wasn’t that great, I’m going to take the story information I generated and put it into Notion so I can use Future Fiction Academy’s Rexy Word Banger to try other LLMs to generate the first two scenes and compare the writing.

After running through the following LLMs:

Gpt-3.5-turbo

Gpt-3.5-turbo-16k

Gpt-4

Gemini-pro-1.5

Claude-haiku

Claude-sonnet

Claude-2.0

Claude-opus

[All temperature and TopP was left at 1.0]

I believe Claude 3 Opus was the best writer, sticking to the plan and sounding most interesting. It was also the most expensive, costing $0.43 for two scenes. Claude 2.0 was next best, in my opinion. It wrote more words than Opus and had better banter. However, it tended to keep writing past the scene, even though I told it not to (I think if I lowered the temperature and TopP to .7 and .7, it might have done better). Two Claude 2.0 scenes cost $0.22, half as much as Opus.

Usually, I like Sonnet, but for some reason, the first chapter was really terse. I even tried it twice but only got about 700-800 words. The second chapter was over 2000 words. Sonnet does have a snarkier voice than the other LLMs, and I enjoy the banter and colorful words. Sonnet’s cost is about $0.08 for two scenes.

The rest of the LLMs, Claude Haiku, the OpenAI GPTs, and gemini-pro-1.5 had very terse writing styles that didn’t work. The OpenAI models tended to do more telling and less dialogue. Haiku had some fun dialogue but rushed through the scene. Gemini-pro-1.5 had deeper insight and followed directions better, but it was too on the nose and rushed through the scenes. These LLMs produced less than 1000 words per scene. Costs for the two scenes. Haiku: $0.005 or half a penny, gpt3.5-turbo: $0.02, gemini-pro-1.5: $0.16 and gpt4: $0.33

If you want to see how the fine tunes differ, I ran two. One was trained in romantic comedy, and the other one in my general writing style. All fine-tune scenes have a temperature of 0.7 and TopP of 0.7.

You can see the writing samples on the “AI Fiction Mastery Supplemental Materials” Page https://adorable-porcupine.super.site/ai-fiction-mastery-supplemental-material ⁠3

Everything is unedited and exactly what came out of the LLMs.

​[ ​45​ ]​

SAMPLE AI WRITING - EXCERPT FROM SIT, STAY, HEEL: TRAINING YOUR BOYFRIEND LIKE A PRO

UNEDITED - From Claude 3.0 - Sonnet 2024-03-27 05:49:51.869757

Here is Chapter 9 - “Grooming and Hygiene: Keeping Your Boyfriend Clean and Presentable” in Penelope Paws’ voice, at around 3000 words:

Grooming and Hygiene: Keeping Your Boyfriend Clean and Presentable

Ooh, this is going to be a fun one, friends! As we all know, a well-trained boyfriend is a presentable boyfriend. And there’s no better way to ensure your man looks pawfect than by giving him a complete grooming makeover. I’m talking a full overhaul – new wardrobe, trendy haircut, and an upgraded skincare routine that will have him glowing brighter than a show poodle at Westminster.

Now, I know what you’re thinking: “But Penelope, isn’t that kind of…I don’t know…controlling?” To which I say, pish posh! This isn’t about molding your boyfriend into someone he’s not. It’s about bringing out his inner alpha dog and helping him put his best paw forward.

“You keep telling yourself that, Pen,” Mr. Snuffles chimes in, puffing away on his toy cigar. “We both know this is just another one of your harebrained schemes to play master groomer.”

Oh hush, you snarky little fuzzball. What do you know about cultivating a refined yet approachable look? With a few strategic snips and some product, we’ll have Max looking like a rugged yet refined model, ready to strut down the catwalk of life!

I declare this with all the determination of a samurai warrior, wielding my fabric shears like a sacred sword. Mr. Snuffles simply snorts and mutters something about me turning Max into a “poodle-headed Casanova” when he should be embracing his natural “inner mutt.”

Ignoring his jabs, I set my plan into motion…

Step 1) The Wardrobe Upgrade

The first stop on our makeover mission? The mall, of course! There’s no better way to revamp a guy’s look than with some fresh, stylish threads. So I grabbed Max by the hand (well, tried to – he kept pulling away like I was dragging him to obedience school) and marched him straight to the trendiest menswear boutique in town.

“Pen, I don’t know about this…” Max mumbles, eyeing the racks of slim-fit pants and crisp button-downs with trepidation. “Can’t I just wear my old t-shirts and jeans?”

“Nonsense, my scruffily-dressed stud muffin!” I trill, holding up a sleek blazer that screams ‘sophisticated yet approachable.’ “We’re going to transform you into an absolute heartthrob. The ladies won’t be able to resist your dapper new look!”

Max tugs at the collar, grimacing. I can already see the doubt flickering in those puppy dog eyes of his. But never fear – that’s where positive reinforcement comes in! After piling his arms high with potential outfits, I lead him to the dressing room and dangle the greatest motivator of all in front of his nose: treats!

“For every ensemble you try on, you get one of these tasty liver snaps,” I coax, giving the bag an enticing shake.

Three hours and seventeen outfits later, Max is practically swimming in a sea of designer duds. Shirt collars dangle askew, pants puddle around his ankles, and I’m pretty sure that jaunty fedora ended up backwards at some point. But hey, at least he got a good protein boost!

“Well, don’t you two look like you just survived a hurricane in a Banana Republic?” Mr. Snuffles drawls from his perch atop a clothing rack, surveying the chaos.

I simply beam at my disheveled but debonair boyfriend. “Don’t you worry, Mr. Skeptical Snuffles – this is all just a trial run. Once we narrow down the options, Max is going to be the best-dressed pup on the block!”

Step 2) The “Haircut of Horrors”

With Max’s new wardrobe hanging neatly in his closet (it took some…persuasion…but we got there), it’s time to move on to the next phase of Operation Dapper Boyfriend: the haircut!

Now, I’ll admit, this one might have been a bit ambitious. In my mind, I envisioned Max with a stylishly tousled look – maybe a little messy but in that “I just rolled out of bed after a night of passionate lovemaking” kind of way. You know, the kind of hairstyle that makes you want to run your fingers through it and…
